Definition of Homonym and Its Distinction from Transferred Term
Defines homonym and distinguishes from transferred term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a homonym from a transferred term by applying the condition of initial position for each meaning.

Core Idea
A homonym needs a separate initial position for each meaning; a transferred term relies on customary usage.

In Islamic legal theory and logic, precise definitions prevent misinterpretation. This passage refines the definition of homonym by introducing the condition that each meaning must have its own 'initial position' (wad' ibtida'i). This criterion excludes transferred terms, which acquire new meanings through customary usage without a new formal position. Understanding this distinction is crucial when analyzing ambiguous words in the Qur'an and Hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identifying the definiendum
Locate the term being defined: al-mushtarak al-lafzi (homonym). Note that the passage focuses on its precise definition.
The definiendum appears early in the Arabic text.
2
Extracting the genus and differentia
The genus is 'a word placed for multiple meanings'. The differentia is that each meaning is assigned by a separate initial position (wad' ibtida'i). This is the core of the definition.
The phrase 'for each of those meanings initially' (لكل واحد من تلك المعانى ابتداء) is the key qualifier.
3
Understanding 'initial position'
Initial position means the word was originally placed for that meaning, not derived from another meaning. Transferred terms lack this because their new meaning comes from customary usage (shuhra) based on appropriateness.
The commentator explains that manqul is excluded by this condition.
4
Comparing the two definitions offered
The commentator presents two definitions: (1) a strict definition requiring a separate position for each meaning; (2) a broader definition including words that by usage lead to multiple meanings. The broad definition encompasses transferred terms if they become ambiguous.
The author concludes that the strict definition is more accurate for true homonyms.
5
Applying the distinction in usul al-fiqh
When a word has multiple meanings, determine if they originated from separate positions or from customary transfer. This affects legal interpretation, e.g., whether a word carries all meanings or only the original.
Example: the word 'ṣalāh' is a transferred term, not a homonym, because its legal meaning came from customary usage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المراد بالوضع الابتدائى هو ان يكون اللفظ موضوعاً لمعناه اولاً اى لا يكون موضوعاً لمعنى آخر ثم وضع له بحسب الاستعمال فيخرج به المنقول عن التعريف و ليس وضعه ابتدائياً بل باعتبار الشهرة. وقال الاستاذ المحقق الشيخ محمد الكرمي سلمه الله في هذا المورد ما هذا لفظه : قوله لكل واحد من تلك المعانى ابتداء - هذا احتراز عما لواشتهر اللفظ الموضوع لمعنى في آخر لمناسبة حتى صار علما في هذا الثانى يستفاد منه بلاقرينة و هو المسمى فى لسان الأصوليين بالوضع التعينى فانه اذا وضع الواضع اللفظ لمعنى واستعمله آخرون فى معنى آخر وتعادل استعمال اللفظ فيهمابان استفيدا منه بلا قرينة تصرف اللفظ من الاول الى الثانى فهذا ليس مما يقال له مشتركه لفظى ان لزم ان يقال في تعريف المشترك اللفظى هو وضع اللفظ لكل واحد من المعانى المتعددة بوضع على حدة فان استعمال اللفظ في معنى لاعن وضع فيه بل عن مناسبة بين المعنى المستعمل فيه والمعنى الموضوع له حتى تعارف استعماله في هذا المستعمل فيه لمناسبة بحيث أصبح المعنى المناسب يستفاد من اللفظ بلا قرينة، لا يقال له وضع أو عن وضع بالضرورة و ان أفاد فائدة المعنى الموضوع له اللفظ ولكن لا يمكن طرد مثل هذا المعنى عن حيز المشتركات اللفظية فهو مثلها في المآل و في النتيجة فالحق ان يقال في تعريف المشترك اللفظي: هو اللفظ الموضوع لكل واحد من المعانى المتعددة بوضع على حدة او اللفظ المنجر به استعمالا إلى هذه النتيجة والمنقول خارج عن التعريف الأول المقول فيه هو اللفظ الموضوع لكل واحد فانه ليس موضوعاً للمعنيين المنقول منه والمنقول اليه وانما هو موضوع للمنقول منه بالبداهة و اما التعريف الثاني الذي ذكرناه فهو يشمله لان اللفظ بالنسبة إلى ما استعمل فيه من معنى المنقول اليه قد اشتهر حتى دل عليه من دون قرينة وافاد فائدة الوضع. كما ان الحق اتباعاً لظواهر اللغة ان المشترك اللفظي لا يقال الاللفظ الذي تناولته يد الوضع فى كل من المعانى التى تستفاد منه وعليه فالمنقول خارج لان يد الوضع لم تتناول المعنى المستعمل فيه المناسبة المعنى الموضوع له قطعاً وقد يكون هو الفارق بين المشتركات والمنقولات.
What is meant by initial position (al-wad' al-ibtida'i) is that the word is placed for its meaning first, i.e., it is not placed for another meaning and then placed for it according to usage. Thus the transferred term (manqul) is excluded from the definition, for its position is not initial but based on customary prevalence. And the professor, the verifying scholar Shaykh Muhammad al-Karmi, may Allah preserve him, said in this regard, his words: His saying: 'for each of those meanings initially' is a precaution against what if the word placed for a meaning becomes prevalent in another meaning due to appropriateness until it becomes a proper name in this second, understood from it without a context (qarina), and this is what is called in the language of usuliyyun 'customary position' (al-wad' al-ta'ini). For if the positer places the word for a meaning and others use it for another meaning, and the usage of the word becomes balanced between them such that both are understood without a context, the word shifts from the first to the second. This is not called a homonym if one must say in the definition of homonym: 'the placement of the word for each of the multiple meanings by a separate position'. For the use of the word for a meaning not by position but by appropriateness between the meaning used and the meaning placed for, until its usage in this used meaning becomes customary due to appropriateness, such that the appropriate meaning is understood from the word without a context, it is not called 'position' or necessarily by position, even if it confers the benefit of the positioned word. But such a meaning cannot be expelled from the domain of homonyms; it is like them in outcome and result. So the truth is to say in the definition of homonym: it is the word placed for each of the multiple meanings by a separate position, or the word that by usage leads to this result. The transferred term (manqul) is excluded from the first definition which says 'the word placed for each', for it is not placed for both the meaning from which it is transferred and the meaning to which it is transferred; rather it is placed for the source meaning by self-evidence. But the second definition we mentioned encompasses it, because the word with respect to what it is used for of the meaning to which it is transferred has become prevalent such that it indicates it without a context and confers the benefit of position. Likewise, the truth, following the apparent meanings of language, is that homonym is only said of a word that the hand of position has encompassed in each of the meanings understood from it. Thus the transferred term is excluded because the hand of position has not encompassed the meaning used through appropriateness to the positioned meaning, absolutely. And this may be the differentiator between homonyms and transferred terms.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage defines the linguistic term 'mushtarak lafzi' (homonym) and distinguishes it from 'manqul' (transferred term). It begins by defining 'initial position' (al-wad' al-ibtida'i): a word is initially placed for one meaning, not later reassigned. This excludes transferred terms, which acquire new meanings through customary prevalence (shuhra). The scholar Shaykh Muhammad al-Karmi then elaborates on the definition: the phrase 'for each of those meanings initially' excludes words that have become commonly used for another meaning due to appropriateness (munasaba) without a new position, known in usul as 'customary position' (al-wad' al-ta'ini). If a word shifts entirely from its original meaning to a new one through common usage, it is not a homonym because homonym requires separate positions for each meaning. However, if such a word becomes ambiguous (indicating both meanings without context), it is considered de facto a homonym. Therefore, the true definition of homonym is either: (1) a word placed for each meaning by a separate position, or (2) a word that by usage leads to that result (multiple meanings without qarina). The transferred term (manqul) is excluded from the first definition but included in the second, because after customary usage it functions like a homonym. The passage concludes that, following linguistic appearances, homonym is only said of a word that has been formally placed for each of its meanings, so transferred terms remain distinct. This distinction is important in usul al-fiqh for interpreting texts.

Key Terms
الوضع الابتدائي (al-wad' al-ibtida'i)
المشترك اللفظي (al-mushtarak al-lafzi)
المنقول (al-manqul)
الوضع التعيني (al-wad' al-ta'ini)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key factor that distinguishes a homonym (mushtarak lafzi) from a transferred term (manqul)?
A) Homonyms have multiple meanings, while transferred terms have only one.
B) Homonyms require a separate initial position for each meaning, while transferred terms acquire new meanings through customary usage without a new formal position.
C) Transferred terms are not used without a context, while homonyms are.
D) Homonyms are only found in Arabic, while transferred terms are cross-linguistic.

Key Takeaway
The definition of homonym requires each meaning to have its own initial position; transferred terms lack this and are distinguished by their origin in customary usage.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the word 'al-wuḍūʾ' (ablution) is a homonym or transferred term. It originally meant 'beauty' but in Islamic law means 'ritual washing'. Using the condition of initial position, argue whether it qualifies as mushtarak lafzi.

Critiquing the Condition of Abandonment in Naql
Author argues that fame and abandonment are not both necessary for naql.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the conditions of fame and abandonment in naql and evaluate whether each is necessary.

Core Idea
Fame alone suffices for naql; abandonment of the original meaning is not required.

In Usul al-Fiqh, the concept of naql—the transfer of a word from its original meaning to a new one—is central to understanding how terms like 'salat' acquired their technical meanings. A common assumption is that naql requires both the word becoming famous in the new meaning and the original meaning being abandoned. This passage challenges that assumption, arguing that fame alone is sufficient. Let's examine why.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its conditions
Find the term 'naql' in the passage and list the two conditions that are discussed: shuhra (fame) and abandon of original meaning.
The base text by al-Taqrib likely asserted both are needed.
2
Extract the critique's claim
The commentator says there is 'no need to stipulate abandonment'. Rewrite in your own words what condition is being dropped and why.
The reason given is that fame and conveying without context already realize naql.
3
Understand the logical structure
The argument is a critical refinement: the author rejects one condition (abandonment) while affirming another (fame). Identify the moved parts.
This is not a full definition but a correction of a condition.
4
Apply to an Islamic term
Take the word 'salat' (original: supplication, transferred: ritual prayer). Does it meet the fame condition? Has the original meaning been completely abandoned?
The original meaning still appears in the Qur'an, so abandonment has not occurred, yet it is a clear case of naql.
5
Evaluate the significance
If abandonment were required, many recognized instances of naql in Islamic law would cease to be considered transferred. This would affect how usul scholars treat terms like 'zakat' and 'hajj'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۸۵) قوله فان اشتهر فى هذا المعنى الثانى وترك استعماله فى المعنى الأول: لاداعى الى ان يشترط في النقل ترك استعمال اللفظ في معناه الموضوع له فان اشتراط اشتهار اللفظ فى المعنى الثاني وافادته له من دون قرينة كاف في تحقق معنى النقل وان لم يهجر اللفظ بالنسبة الى معناه الموضوع له. (التقريب ص ۲۴)
(85) His saying: 'For if it becomes famous in this second meaning and its usage in the first meaning is abandoned': There is no need to stipulate in naql the abandonment of usage of the word in its original meaning. For stipulating the fame of the word in the second meaning and its conveying it without a context is sufficient for the realization of the meaning of naql, even if the word is not abandoned with respect to its original meaning. (al-Taqrib, p. 24)
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage critiques a condition often assumed in discussions of naql (transferred terms). The base text had stated that naql involves both fame in the new meaning and abandonment of the old meaning. The commentator here objects: there is no need to require abandonment. It is enough that the word becomes famous in the second meaning and communicates it without a contextual clue. The word may still be used in its original meaning as well. This is a critical refinement: the author relaxes the condition, allowing naql to coexist with continued use of the original meaning.

Key Terms
النقل
الشهرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The word must become famous in the new meaning and abandon the old meaning
B) The word must become famous in the new meaning, regardless of abandonment
C) The word must be abandoned in the old meaning, regardless of fame
D) The word must have a clear contextual clue in both meanings

Key Takeaway
For a transferred term (naql), it is sufficient that the word becomes famous in the new meaning and conveys it without context; complete abandonment of the original meaning is not required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Refining 'Tark al-Isti'mal' in Naql
Author explains that 'abandonment of usage' means not using literally without context, not total disuse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the precise meaning of 'tark al-isti'mal' in the context of naql and distinguish it from total disuse.

Core Idea
Tark al-isti'mal means not using literally without context, not total abandonment.

In this passage, the author refines the concept of 'abandonment of usage' (tark al-isti'mal) as it applies to transferred terms (manqul). Without this refinement, one might mistakenly think that a transferred term can never be used in its original meaning. The author explains that abandonment simply means not using the word in a literal, context-free manner in its original sense, not that it is never used at all.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being refined
Notice that the author is clarifying the meaning of 'tark al-isti'mal' in the context of naql (transference). What exactly is the term that requires clarification?
The term is 'abandonment of usage' of the original meaning.
2
Understand what it does NOT mean
The author explicitly negates two misunderstandings: first, that abandonment means total disuse (la yusta'mal fihi aslan). The phrase 'hatta yuqal' introduces potential objections based on that misinterpretation.
3
Understand what it DOES mean
The positive definition: 'an la yusta'mal fihi bi-tariq al-haqiqa mujarradan 'an al-qara'in' – not used in a literal way devoid of contextual clues. So the word may still appear in its original meaning if there are contextual indicators (qara'in).
Key: mujarradan 'an al-qara'in (devoid of contextual clues) is the differentia.
4
Apply the definition to the example
Take the word 'salat'. In its original linguistic sense, it means supplication. It was transferred in the religious convention to the specific ritual prayer. Yet, we observe that 'salat' is still used in its original supplication sense in certain contexts (e.g., 'salat al-mala'ika' – supplication of angels). This confirms that tark al-isti'mal is not absolute.
5
Recognize the importance for classification
If tark al-isti'mal meant absolute disuse, then a transferred term with a single transferred meaning would not be considered a word with multiple meanings (mushtarak). The author shows that because the original meaning is still used in a non-literal context, the term does have multiple meanings.
This precision is crucial for the usuli classification of words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۸۶) یعنی: ان المراد من ترك الاستعمال ان لا يستعمل فيه بطريق الحقيقة مجرداً عن القرائن لا ان لا يستعمل فيه اصلاً حتى يقال :اولا ان هذاينا في عد المنقول مطلقا من اقسام متكثر المعنى لانه اذا كان المعنى المنقول اليه واحداً وترك استعماله في المعنى الاول لايكون من اقسام متكثر المعنى فان المراد من المعنى كما سبق المستعمل فيه اللفظ وثانيا : انا ترى بالعيان ان من المنقولات ما يستعمل في المعنى الأول ايضاً كلفظ الصلوة مثلاً فانه و ان كان منقولاً في عرف الشرع الى الاركان المخصوصة لكن قد
(86) Meaning: The intended meaning of 'abandonment of usage' is that it is not used in it in a literal way, devoid of contextual clues; not that it is not used in it at all. So it cannot be said: First, we have counted the transferred term absolutely among the divisions of multiple meaning, because if the transferred meaning is one and its usage in the first meaning is abandoned, it would not be among the divisions of multiple meaning, for the intended meaning of 'meaning' as before is that in which the word is used. And second, we see with the eye that among transferred terms are those that are also used in the first meaning, like the word 'salat' for example, for even though it is transferred in the religious convention to the specific pillars, yet [it has been...]
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage refines the concept of 'tark al-isti'mal' (abandonment of usage) in the context of naql. The author clarifies that 'abandonment' does not mean total disuse, but rather that the word is no longer used in its original meaning in a literal sense without contextual clues. The reason for this clarification is to avoid two problems: (1) if abandonment meant total disuse, then a transferred term with a single transferred meaning would not be classified as a word with multiple meanings, since the original meaning is not used at all; (2) empirically, many transferred terms like 'salat' (prayer) are still used in their original linguistic meaning (supplication) even after being transferred to the religious technical meaning. Thus, abandonment is a matter of literal, context-free usage, not absolute disuse.

Key Terms
ترك الاستعمال (tark al-isti'mal): Abandonment of usage; not using a word in its original meaning literally without contextual clues. [Note: This passage refines the term to avoid the misunderstanding of absolute disuse.]
الصلاة (al-salat): Example of a transferred term: originally meaning supplication, transferred in religious convention to the specific ritual prayer. [Note: Classic example in usul.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what does 'tark al-isti'mal' mean in the context of naql?
A) The word is never used in the original meaning under any circumstances
B) The word is not used in the original meaning in a literal sense without contextual clues
C) The word is only used in the transferred meaning
D) The word is abandoned by the community

Key Takeaway
Abandonment of usage in naql does not mean total disuse, but rather the absence of literal, context-free usage in the original meaning.

Four Types of Words: Haqiqah, Majaz, Manqool, and Muhmallāt
Author defines four types of words and provides examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four types of words (muhmallāt, manqool, haqiqah, majaz) and classify Islamic terms accordingly.

Core Idea
Words are not static; they can be literal, metaphorical, transferred, or abandoned.

In this passage from a classical logic gloss, the author defines four categories of words based on their usage: neglected terms (muhmallāt), transferred terms (manqool), literal (haqiqah), and metaphorical (majaz). Understanding this classification is essential for interpreting Islamic legal and theological texts, where the same word may carry different meanings across contexts.

Guided Path
1
Locate the four types in the Arabic passage
Find the names of the four categories: المهمات, المنقول, الحقيقة, المجاز.
2
Understand the definition of each type
For each, note the key characteristic: muhmallāt have meanings assigned but never used; manqool have been transferred completely away from original; haqiqah used in original; majaz used in non-original with a connection.
3
Identify the examples given in the passage
The author gives an example of manqool as the second type. Note that the fourth type includes haqiqah, majaz, and manqool that still retains its original use.
4
Apply the classification to a new term from Islamic sciences
Take the term 'صلاة' (salat) and determine its status: It is a manqool transferred from the literal meaning 'prayer' to the specific ritual prayer, but it has not completely abandoned the original meaning (since it can still mean 'connection' in some contexts). So it fits the fourth type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الدلالات المهمات موضوعة لها عندهم لكنها لم تستعمل فيها اصلاً فهى مجازات بلا حقيقة عندهم، ومثال القسم الثاني منها هو المنقول الذى حصل له انتقالات متعددة من اهل الصناعات المختلفة الى المعانى المتغايرة بحيث ترك استعماله فى المعنى الاول الموضوع له بالكلية، فهو مثال لما وضع الجميع معانيها باوضاع متعددة ثانوية ومثال القسم الثالث المهمات ايضاً عند المتأخرين منهم وقد سبق الاشارة اليه في الحاشية السابقة منا ومثال القسم الرابع منها الحقيقة والمجاز والمنقول الذى لم يترك استعماله في المعنى الاصلى بالكلية (میرزا محمد علی (ره)
Concerning significations: the neglected meanings are assigned to them but they are not used in them at all, so they are metaphors without literal meaning according to them. An example of the second type is the transferred term (manqool) that has undergone multiple transfers by people of different crafts to diverse meanings such that its use in the first originally assigned meaning is completely abandoned. Thus it is an example of what is assigned to multiple meanings with multiple secondary assignments. An example of the third type is the neglected type also according to later scholars, and reference to it has preceded in our previous gloss. An example of the fourth type is the literal, metaphorical, and transferred term that has not completely abandoned its use in the original meaning. (Mirza Muhammad Ali (ra))
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage classifies words into four types based on their usage. The author begins by mentioning 'المهمات' (the neglected ones): words that have meanings assigned to them but are never actually used, making them metaphors without a literal sense. The second type is the 'منقول' (transferred term) that has shifted completely from its original meaning to new ones, such that the original is no longer used. The third type is also 'المهمات' but according to later scholars, already discussed earlier. The fourth type includes literal, metaphorical, and transferred terms that still retain their original usage. This classification helps in understanding the dynamics of word meanings in Islamic logic and linguistics.

Key Terms
المهمات
المنقول
الحقيقة
المجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The classification of words into four types—muhmallāt, manqool, haqiqah, and majaz—provides a framework for analyzing how word meanings shift in Islamic texts, which is essential for accurate interpretation.

Condition for Naql: Prevalence over Abandonment
Hashiya author refines condition for naql

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the refined condition for naql (prevalence in new meaning) and the common misunderstanding that requires complete abandonment of the original meaning, and apply this distinction to the example of 'salat'.

Core Idea
Prevalence in the new meaning, not abandonment of the old, defines naql.

In usul al-fiqh, naql (transfer of meaning) is a key concept. Often one might think that for a word to be considered transferred to a new meaning, the original meaning must be completely abandoned. However, the hashia author refines this condition, arguing that what matters is ishtihar (fame) in the new meaning such that the word is understood without a contextual clue, even if the original meaning is still used with a clue. This lesson unpacks that refinement using the example of 'salat'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition being refined
The base text states: 'So if it becomes famous in this second meaning and its use in the first meaning is abandoned.' The question is what 'abandonment of use' means. The hashia author clarifies that it means not using it literally without contextual clues, not absolute non-use.
This distinction is crucial to avoid misinterpretation.
2
Distinguish two interpretations of 'abandonment'
Absolute abandonment: the word is never used in the first meaning. Restricted abandonment: the word is not used in the first meaning in a literal manner without contextual clues (qarain). The hashia author endorses the latter.
3
Examine the two objections avoided by this refinement
If absolute abandonment were required, two objections arise: (1) manqul words would not be considered multi-meaning words if only one new meaning exists and the old is abandoned; (2) we observe that some manqul words like 'salat' are still used in the original meaning (supplication) in some contexts.
These objections show why absolute abandonment is not required.
4
Apply the refined condition to 'salat'
The word 'salat' originally meant supplication but in the legal convention refers to specific pillars. It is still used for supplication but with a contextual clue. So it is manqul because it is prevalent in the new meaning without needing a clue.
Thus, 'salat' satisfies the condition of ishtihar.
5
Summarize the dual reality of manqul words
With respect to the original convention, the word is literal in the first meaning and metaphorical in the second. With respect to the legal convention, the opposite holds. This dual status is consistent with the refined condition.

(محمد علی) (۸۷) قوله و ان لم يستعمل ( يشتهرج فى الثانى ولم يهجز في الأول اى لم يستعمل في الثاني استعما يفيده من غير قرينه والا فاللفظ في الحقيقة والمجاز مستعمل في المعنى المجازى بلاريب ولكن استعمالا بقرينة لا من دون قرينة كما فى المنقول. (التقريب ص ۲۴)
(85) His saying: 'So if it becomes famous in this second meaning and its use in the first meaning is abandoned': There is no need to stipulate in transfer (naql) that the word's use in its assigned meaning be abandoned. Indeed, stipulating the fame of the word in the second meaning and its conveying that meaning without a context (qarīnah) is sufficient for the realization of the meaning of transfer, even if the word is not abandoned with respect to its assigned meaning. (al-Taqrib p. 24) (86) Meaning: What is meant by 'abandonment of use' is that it is not used in it in a literal way, devoid of contextual clues, not that it is not used in it at all. This is to avoid two objections: first, that we would be counting the transferred term absolutely as one of the types of multi-meaning words, because if the meaning transferred to is one, and its use in the first meaning is abandoned, then it would not be among the types of multi-meaning words, for what is meant by 'meaning' is, as previously, that in which the word is used; and second, we see with our eyes that among transferred terms, some are used in the first meaning as well, such as the word 'salāt', for example. For although it is transferred in the legal convention to specific pillars, it is also used in their convention in the first meaning, namely supplication. The summary of the rebuttal is clear: the word 'salāt', even if used in the legal convention for absolute supplication as well, is not used in a literal way devoid of contextual clues. Rather, it is such that the second meaning, i.e., the specific pillars, springs to mind when uttered in the legal convention without contextual clues. So the transferred term, with respect to the original convention, is literal in the first meaning and metaphorical in the second, and with respect to the second convention, the opposite. So understand. (Muhammad Ali) (87) His saying: 'And if it is not used (famous) in the second and not abandoned in the first': i.e., it is not used in the second in a usage that conveys it without a contextual clue. Otherwise, the word in literal and metaphorical is used in the metaphorical meaning without doubt, but with a contextual clue, not without a clue, as in the transferred term. (al-Taqrib p. 24)
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the condition for naql (transfer of meaning). The author argues that the key is not complete abandonment of the original meaning, but rather the fame (ishtihar) of the word in the new meaning such that it is understood without a contextual clue. They use the example of 'salāt' (prayer) which originally meant 'supplication' but in Islamic legal convention came to refer to the specific ritual acts. Yet 'salāt' is still used for supplication in certain contexts, but with a contextual clue. Therefore, the transfer is not negated. The passage also clarifies that 'abandonment of use' means not being used literally without contextual clues, not absolute non-use. This refined understanding prevents objections that would otherwise invalidate the category of manqul.

الاشتهار
القرينة
العرف

Key Takeaway
The condition for naql is ishtihar (fame) in the new meaning, not the abandonment of the original meaning; the latter is only relevant to literal usage without clues.

Defining Haqiqah and Majaz: The Perspective Condition
Author defines haqiqah as word used in its assigned meaning qua that meaning, and majaz as word used in other than its assigned meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define haqiqah and majaz with the author's rigorous conditions, and identify the role of convention in determining whether a word is literal or metaphorical.

Core Idea
A word is haqiqah only when used in its assigned meaning from the perspective of that assignment.

This lesson presents the precise definitions of haqiqah (literal) and majaz (metaphorical) as given by the author. These definitions are fundamental in Islamic legal theory and Quranic interpretation. The author's key innovation is the phrase 'from the perspective that it is such' (min haythu huwa kadhalik), which excludes metaphorical uses that happen to share the same meaning but in a different conventional context. By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply these definitions to classify words in Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definiens of haqiqah
Find the Arabic definition: 'al-haqiqah huwa al-lafdh al-musta'mal fima wudi'a lahu min haythu huwa kadhalik'. Identify three parts: the word, the usage in its assigned meaning, and the perspective condition.
The term 'haqiqah' itself is the definiendum.
2
Understand what each part excludes
'Used' excludes words not employed at all. 'In what it was assigned for' excludes words used in a meaning they were not originally designated to convey (including mistakes). 'From the perspective that it is such' excludes words used in their assigned meaning but in a different conventional system (e.g., a grammarian using 'fi'l' for event when in Arabic it means verb).
This last condition is crucial for distinguishing haqiqah from majaz in cross-conventional usage.
3
Examine the examples given
Analyze the author's examples: 'I saw a lion (asad) shooting' – 'asad' is used for a brave man (not animal), so it's majaz. 'Take this horse' pointing to a book – incorrect usage, not haqiqah. The grammarian's use of 'fi'l' for event: though it matches the linguistic assignment, the convention of grammar overrides, so it's not haqiqah in that context.
These examples illustrate the boundaries of the definition.
4
Define majaz
The author briefly defines majaz at the end: 'al-majaz huwa al-lafdh al-musta'mal fi ghayri ma wudi'a lahu fi istilah al-musta'mil'. So majaz is a word used in a meaning other than its assigned meaning in the convention of the user. Note that 'istilah' (convention) is key – the same word can be haqiqah in one convention and majaz in another.
This definition sets the stage for deeper discussions in usul al-fiqh and tafsir.

(88) So al-haqiqah is the word used in what it was assigned for, from the perspective that it is such. So what is excluded by 'used' is that which is not used in any meaning at all, for it is not called haqiqah in the terminology, just as it is not called majaz. And by our saying 'in what it was assigned for' excludes that which is not used in what it was assigned for, neither in the convention in which communication occurs nor in another, like the word 'asad' (lion) in our saying: 'I saw an asad shooting' or 'in the bath' as it is said: that it excludes the word used in other than its assigned meaning by mistake, like your saying: 'Take this horse' pointing to a book. And it is not hidden that this is based on usage being general to correct and other. But if we say that what is meant by it is that which is in accordance with the law of language and assignment, based on it being the most complete individuals and most famous, then what is excluded by the condition of 'used' and the condition of 'perspective' is the metaphor that is used in what it was assigned for, but not in the convention in which communication occurs, but in another convention, like the word 'fi'l' (verb) when the speaker, who knows grammar, uses it for absolute event. For although it is true that it is a word used in what it was assigned for, because it is assigned for it in the language, this usage of it is not from the perspective that it is assigned for it, but from the perspective that it is something else containing a relation. Then 'haqiqah' originally is fa'il in the sense of fa'il (active participle) from haqqa al-shay' (the thing became established), or in the sense of maf'ul (passive participle) from haqqaqtu al-shay' (I established the thing). It was transferred to mean the word that is established or established in its original place, and the ta' is for transference on both views. And the author of al-Miftah said it is for femininity as well. As for the first view, it is apparent because fa'il in the sense of fa'il agrees with the masculine and feminine, whether it is applied to its described noun or not. So it is said: rajul zarif and imra'ah zarifah, and I saw zarifan and zarifatan. And as for His saying: '... who will revive bones while they are decayed (ramim)', 'ramim' is not fa'il in the sense of fa'il, but rather it is a noun for decayed bones, as stated in al-Kashshaf, or fa'il in the sense of maf'ul from rahamtuhu. As for the second view, there is some subtlety, because fa'il in the sense of maf'ul is the same for masculine and feminine: one says rajul jarih and imra'ah jarih. Unless the word 'haqiqah' before transference to the conventional meaning is assumed to be an adjective for a feminine noun not applied to its described noun, because fa'il in the sense of maf'ul when it is not applied to its described noun agrees with the masculine and feminine to avoid confusion: one says marartu bi-jarih and jarihah and qatil and qatilah. And it is not hidden that on this view, haqiqah must be restricted to the singular word, because the described noun here is the word (kalimah), and the well-known view is that just as it can be singular, it can be compound, unless it is claimed that it is restricted to it according to him, as appears from his definition of it as 'the word used in what it was assigned for without interpretation'. Regardless, there is unnecessary difficulty. (Muhammad Ali) (89) His saying: 'It is called majaz': So al-majaz is the word used in other than what it was assigned for in the convention of the user.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage provides a rigorous definition of haqiqah (literal) and majaz (metaphorical). The author defines haqiqah as a word used in its assigned meaning from the very perspective that it is assigned. This excludes words that are never used, or used incorrectly, or used in a different convention. The definition also distinguishes between usage in the same convention versus another. The author then delves into the etymology of the word 'haqiqah', discussing whether it is derived from fa'il or maf'ul, and the feminization with ta'. He analyzes the views of scholars like the author of al-Miftah and quotes Quranic examples. The passage ends with a brief definition of majaz as a word used in other than its assigned meaning in the convention of the user. This is a foundational definition for understanding the classification of words.

Distinguishing Compound and Simple Genera via Tamam al-Mushtarak
Defines 'tamam al-mushtarak' with compound/simple genera distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish compound and simple genera by analyzing whether 'tamam al-mushtarak' consists of multiple shared attributes or a single common nature.

Core Idea
The total of what is shared is either a collection or a single essence.

This passage from Yazdi's Hashiya unpacks the logical term 'tamam al-mushtarak' — the sum total of what species under a genus have in common. By studying how this term applies to both compound genera (like 'animal') and simple genera (like 'substance'), you will learn to recognize the logical structure that underpins all definitions in Islamic logic. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the five universals when constructing definitions in usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase being explained
The commentator begins by analyzing the base text phrase 'fain kana tamam al-mushtarak'. Recognize that 'tamam al-mushtarak' means 'the total of what is shared', and the 'lam' in 'al-mushtarak' indicates the genus.
2
Distinguish the two cases
The author says: 'if what is shared is multiple' (compound genus) and 'if it is not multiple' (simple genus). Note that in compound genera, there are multiple common attributes; in simple genera, only one common nature.
3
Analyze the examples
For compound: 'animal' — the shared features between human and horse (e.g., life, sensation) form the total. For simple: 'substance' — the single shared nature between intellect and human is the total.
The examples are from classical Islamic logic; you can substitute other species to test understanding.
4
Note the alternative interpretation
Some restrict 'tamam al-mushtarak' only to compound genera. The commentator presents this view but does not fully adopt it. Recognize the scholarly disagreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله فان كان تمام المشترك اى تمام المشترك فيه واللام فيه للجنس، فيعم ما اذا كان المشترك فيه متعدداً كما في الاجناس المركبة كالحيوان مثلاً فانه مجموع المشتركات بين الانسان و الفرس مثلاً بمعنى ان ليس بينهما مشترك الا وهو جزء منه و مالم يكن متعدداً كما في الاجناس البسيطة كالجوهر فانه تمام المشترك بين العقل والانسان مثلاً بمعنى ان ليس بينها مشترك الا اياه. ومنهم من فسره بما يختص بالقسم الاول حيث قال المراد بتمام المشترك تمام الاجزاء المشتركة بينهما
His statement: 'For if it is the totality of the common (tamam al-mushtarak),' i.e., the totality of what is shared in, and the 'lam' in it is for the genus. So it includes when what is shared is multiple, as in compound genera like 'animal' for example, for it is the sum of the commonalities between 'human' and 'horse', meaning there is no commonality between them except that it is a part of it. And if it is not multiple, as in simple genera like 'substance', then it is the totality of the common between 'intellect' and 'human' for example, meaning there is no commonality between them except it. And among them are those who interpret it as restricted to the first division, where they said: 'The intention by tamam al-mushtarak is the totality of the common parts between them.'
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'tamam al-mushtarak' (the totality of what is shared) in the context of logical genera. The commentator begins by analyzing the phrase: 'tamam al-mushtarak' means the sum total of what is shared among the species under a genus. The 'lam' in 'al-mushtarak' indicates the genus. Two cases are distinguished: (1) When the shared aspects are multiple, as in a compound genus like 'animal'—it is the collection of all common features between humans and horses, etc. (2) When the shared aspect is single, as in a simple genus like 'substance'—it is the single common nature between intellect and human. Some authorities restrict the term to the first case. This explanation clarifies the logical structure of genus and species, showing how genera can be either collections of attributes or a single essence.

Key Terms
تمام المشترك
الأجناس المركبة
الأجناس البسيطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Tamam al-mushtarak is the sum total of what species share under a genus, and it can be either a collection of multiple attributes (compound genus) or a single nature (simple genus).

Objection and Answer on Complete Shared Part and Simple Genera
Objection and answer on genus definition with simple genera

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyse a dialectical exchange about the definition of genus and identify how the complete shared part (tamam al-mushtarak) functions in different comparative contexts.

Core Idea
A term can be a genus in one comparison and a part of a genus in another—context determines completeness.

In this passage, the author presents an objection against the definition of genus as 'the complete shared part' and then answers it. The objection concerns simple genera like 'growing body' and 'absolute body', which seem to be shared but not complete when comparing certain species. The answer reveals that the same term can be the complete shared part for a different pair. This lesson walks you through the logical structure of this objection and its resolution, highlighting the contextual nature of genus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition under attack
The definition of genus being tested is 'the complete shared part between different realities'. Note that this is the standard definition in Islamic logic.
2
Extract the objector's counterexample
The objector points to simple genera like 'growing body' and 'absolute body'. In the comparison between man and horse, 'growing body' is not the complete shared part—animal is. Therefore, if genus is defined as the complete shared part, then 'growing body' would not be a genus, which is false.
3
Understand the objector's consequence
The objector argues that if such simple genera are not genera, they would fall under the differentia (fasl), which is absurd.
4
Analyse the answerer's response
The answerer shows that the objector's pair selection is arbitrary. Compare man and tree: 'growing body' is the complete shared part. Each simple genus is the complete shared part for some pair.
5
Generalise the insight
Genus is relative to the pair of species under comparison. A term can be a genus in one context and a part of a genus in another, depending on what is being compared.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كالحيوان فانه مجموع الجوهر والجسم النامي والحساس و المتحرك بالارادة و هى اجزاء مشتركة بين الانسان والفرس فينتقض التعريف بالاجناس البسيطة فتأمل. فان قلت ان هذا غير شامل للاجناس البعيدة فانها ليست تمام المشترك بل جزئه كالجسم النامي بالنسبة الى الانسان و الفرس فان تمام المشترك بينهما هو الحيوان والجسم النامي جزئه و كالجسم المطلق بالنسبة اليهما أو اليهما و الى الشجر فان تمام المشترك على الأول هو الحيوان و على الثانى هو الجسم النامي و ليس الجسم المطلق الاجزء منها فيلزم ان يكون الاجناس البعيدة داخلة فى حيز قوله: «والا فهو الفصل» كما هو ظاهر لمن له ادنى مسكة. قلت ان الجسم النامى و ان لم يكن بالنسبة الى الانسان و الفرس تمام المشترك ، الا انه تمام المشترك بالنسبة الى الانسان والشجر و كذا الجسم المطلق فانه وان كان بالنسبة الى الانسان والفرس او اليهما والى الشجر ليس بتمام المشترك ، الا انه بالنسبة الى احدها والحجر تمام المشترك و هكذا فتأمل. (ميرزا محمد علی)
Like 'animal', for it is the sum of substance, growing body, sentient, and moving by will, and these are parts shared between man and horse, so the definition is refuted by the simple genera. Ponder. For you say: This does not include the distant genera, for they are not the complete shared part but a part of it, like growing body with respect to man and horse; the complete shared part between them is animal, and growing body is a part of it. And like absolute body with respect to them or to them and to tree; the complete shared part in the first is animal, and in the second is growing body, and absolute body is not a part of them. Hence, the distant genera would fall under his statement: 'Otherwise it is the differentia', as is apparent to anyone with the slightest intelligence. I say: Growing body, even if it is not the complete shared part with respect to man and horse, yet it is the complete shared part with respect to man and tree. Likewise absolute body, even if with respect to man and horse or to them and tree it is not the complete shared part, yet with respect to one of them and stone it is the complete shared part, and so on. Ponder. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange (naqd wa jawab) regarding the definition of genus, specifically the claim that the definition of genus as 'the complete shared part between different realities' is refuted by simple genera like growing body or absolute body that are shared but not complete. The objector argues that these simple genera are not the complete shared part (tamam al-mushtarak) between two things, but only a part of it. For example, between man and horse, the complete shared part is animal, while growing body is only a part of animal. Therefore, if we define genus as the complete shared part, distant genera like growing body would not be genera but would fall under the differentia (fasl), which is absurd. The answerer (Mirza Muhammad Ali) responds by pointing out that the same simple genera can be the complete shared part when compared to other pairs. For instance, growing body is the complete shared part between man and tree, even though it is not so between man and horse. Thus, the objection is answered by considering the relativity of the pair. This shows the nuanced nature of logical definitions, where a term can be a genus in one context and a part of a genus in another.

The Complete Shared Part (Tamam al-Mushtarak)
Sheikh Abdur Rahim clarifies the meaning of 'complete shared part' as that which leaves no common part outside.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the complete shared part between two species and distinguish it from partial shared parts.

Core Idea
The complete shared part is the most comprehensive common element containing all other common elements.

Sheikh Abdur Rahim explains a key concept in definition theory: the complete shared part (tamam al-mushtarak). This is the highest common genus which encompasses all common properties, essential for precise definition. Understanding it ensures we use the correct genus in logical and legal definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two species
Find the two things being compared. In the passage, it is man and horse.
2
List all common properties
List every property shared by both species. For man and horse: substance, body, growing, sentient, volitional mover.
These are all shared, but some are more general.
3
Determine the most comprehensive
Find which common property includes all the others. 'Animal' includes substance, body, growing, sentience, and voluntary motion. It is the complete shared part because no common property lies outside it.
Any other common property, like 'sentient', is only a part of 'animal'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الشيخ عبد الرحيم (ره): المراد بتمام المشترك الذى لا يكون ورائه جزء مشترك بينهما اي جزء مشترك لا يكون جزء مشترك خارجاً عنه بل كل جزء مشترك بينهما يكون اما نفس ذلك الجزء أو جزء منه كالحيوان فانه تمام المشترك بين الانسان والفرس اذ لا جزء مشترك الا وهو اما نفس الحيوان او جزء منه كالجوهر والجسم النامى و الحساس و المتجرك بالارادة فكل منها وان كان مشتركا بين الانسان والفرس، الا انه ليس تمام المشترك بينهما بل بعضه وانما تمام المشترك بينهما هو الحيوان المشتمل على الكل. (۶۶)
And Sheikh Abdur Rahim (may God have mercy on him) said: What is meant by 'the complete shared part' is that beyond which there is no shared part between them, i.e., a shared part such that no shared part is outside it, rather every shared part between them is either that very part or a part of it. Like animal, for it is the complete shared part between man and horse, since there is no shared part except that it is either animal itself or a part of it, like substance, growing body, sentient, and moving by will. Each of these, even if it is shared between man and horse, is not the complete shared part between them but some of it; rather, the complete shared part between them is animal, which encompasses all.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory expansion (sharh) by Sheikh Abdur Rahim on the concept of 'the complete shared part' (tamam al-mushtarak), which is crucial for defining genus. He defines it as a shared part that leaves no common part outside itself; every common part is either identical to it or a component of it. Using the example of animal between man and horse, he illustrates that animal is the complete shared part because every common property (substance, growing body, sentience, voluntary motion) is either animal itself or a part of animal. The individual properties like growing body are shared but are only parts of the whole, not the complete shared part. This distinction ensures that genus is the comprehensive shared reality, not a partial one.

Key Takeaway
The complete shared part is the most comprehensive common element that contains all other common elements; using any lesser part leads to an incomplete definition.

Understanding the Two Uses of 'Ma Huwa' in Defining Genus and Species
Commentator analyzes the two uses of 'ma huwa' and their implications for defining genus and species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'ma al-shariha' and 'ma al-haqiqa' and explain why only the latter is used in the standard definitions of genus and species.

Core Idea
The 'ma' in 'ma huwa' must be essential, not explanatory, to define genus and species correctly.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies a crucial distinction: the word 'ma' in the logical question 'ma huwa' can be either explanatory or essential. This distinction has direct implications for the definitions of genus and species. If we mistakenly use the explanatory 'ma', accidents could be included, which would undermine the precision of these logical categories. The passage then explores how the answer to 'ma huwa' varies depending on whether the question is about a particular, a universal, or a collection of things.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of 'ma'
The passage states that 'ma' is of two kinds: 'ma al-shariha' (explanatory) used to ask for the meaning of a name, and 'ma al-haqiqa' (essential) used to ask for the true quiddity. Notice the different purposes of each.
The explanatory 'ma' yields nominal definitions; the essential 'ma' yields real definitions.
2
Understand the consequence for answers to 'ma huwa'
When asked with 'ma al-haqiqa', the answer is the genus or species. When asked with 'ma al-shariha', accidents may be given as answers. This is why the definitions of genus and species must refer specifically to the essential 'ma'.
If accidents could be answers, then the definitions would incorrectly include accidental properties.
3
Apply the distinction to the definitions of genus and species
The standard definitions state: genus is 'predicated of many differing realities in answer to ma huwa', and species is 'predicated of many agreeing realities in answer to ma huwa'. The commentator argues that 'ma huwa' here must mean the essential 'ma', otherwise accidents would fall under genus and species.
This refinement ensures that genus and species are based on essential, not accidental, commonality.
4
Evaluate the commentator's resolution
To avoid using a homonym without context, it is claimed that in logical terminology, 'ma' has become specialized to 'ma al-haqiqa'. The passage then elaborates on how the answer varies: for a single particular (e.g., Zayd) the species is given; for a universal (e.g., man) the complete definition; for multiple agreeing things the species; for multiple differing things the genus.
This shows how the answer depends on the scope and nature of the subject of the question.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۶۹) قوله ما هو سؤال عن تمام :الحقيقة لما كانت كلمة «ما» على قسمين: «ما» الشارحة و التي تستعمل لطلب شرح الاسم و بیان مفهومه وانه لاتى معنى وضع و «ما» الحقيقة و هي التي تستعمل لطلب الماهية الحقيقية وكان اذا سئل عن الاشياء المتفقة الحقايق او المختلفة الحقايق بما الحقيقة يقع النوع أو الجنس في الجواب و اذا سئل عنها بما الشارحة جازان يقع العرضيات في الجواب كما صرح بذلك المصنف فى شرح التلخيص فلا يصح تعريف الجنس بانه المقول على الكثرة المختلفة الحقايق في جواب ماهو و تعريف النوع بأنه المقول على الكثرة المتفقة الحقايق في جواب ما هو والايلزم ان يكون العرضيات داخلة تحت الجنس و النوع الصدق تعريفهما عليها، اشار المحشى الى ان المراد من كلمة «ما» ما الحقيقة فقط لا مطلق «ما» حتى يرد ما ذكر ولا يخفى انه على ذلك يلزم استعمال اللفظ المشترك في التعريف من غير قرينة معينة، اللهم الا ان يدعى ان كلمة «ما» و ان كانت بحسب اصل اللغة تستعمل في المعنيين الا انها اختصت في اصطلاح اهل الميزان بـ «ما» الحقيقة. (میرزا محمد علی (ره) وقال استاذنا الاعظم الشيخ محمد الكرمى دامت بركاته في تحقيق المقام ما هذا لفظه): قوله «ما هو سؤال عن تمام الحقيقة»: ما هو تستعمل مرة في شرح اسم المسؤل عنه ويسأل بها عما تحت «ماهو اللفظ من مفهوم اسمى فيقال: ما العنب ؟ فيجاب بانه فاكهة من الفواكه، وتستعمل ثانية في السؤال عن تمام حقيقة الشيء فان اقتصر فى السؤال على ذكر امر واحد مثل قولنا ما زيد؟» كان السؤال عن تمام الماهية المختصة به، فيقع النوع فى الجواب: (انسان) فان الانسان تمام ماهية زيد المختصة به، هذا اذا كان الامر الواحد امراً شخصياً، وان كان الامر الواحد المذكور فى السؤال حقيقة كلية نحو: ما الانسان؟ وقع فى الجواب الحد التام: حيوان ناطق)، و ان جمع في السؤال بين امور ،متعددة، كان السؤال عن تمام الماهية المشتركة بين تلك الأمور، ثم تلك الامور المتعددة ان كانت متفقة الحقيقة مثل زيد وعمرو و بكر و خاند، كان المسئول عنه تمام الحقيقة المتفقة المتحدة في تلك الامور فيقع النوع ايضاً في الجواب وان كانت مختلفة الحقيقة كان المسئول عنه تمام الحقيقة المشتركة بين تلك الحقائق المختلفة، وقد عرفت ان التمام الذاتي المشترك بين الحقائق المختلفة هو الجنس، فيقع الجنس في الجواب، فالجنس لابد ان يقع جواباً عن
(69) His statement: 'Ma huwa is a question about the complete essence.' Since the word 'ma' is of two types: 'ma al-shariha' (explanatory ma) which is used to request an explanation of the name and to express its concept, and that it is of such-and-such a meaning; and 'ma al-haqiqa' (essential ma) which is used to request the true quiddity. And when things of agreeing or differing realities are asked about with 'ma al-haqiqa', the species or genus occurs in the answer. When asked about them with 'ma al-shariha', it is permissible that accidents occur in the answer, as stated by the author in Sharh al-Talkhis. Therefore, it is not correct to define genus as 'that which is predicated of many differing realities in answer to 'ma huwa'', and to define species as 'that which is predicated of many agreeing realities in answer to 'ma huwa'', for then accidents would be included under genus and species with respect to the truth of their definition applying to them. The commentator pointed out that what is meant by the word 'ma' is only 'ma al-haqiqa', not absolute 'ma', such that the above objection would not arise. And it is not hidden that on that basis it would necessitate using a homonym in the definition without a clarifying context, unless it is claimed that the word 'ma', although in its original linguistic usage is used in both meanings, it has become specific in the terminology of the people of the scale (logicians) to 'ma al-haqiqa'. (Mirza Muhammad Ali (may God have mercy on him), and our greatest teacher Sheikh Muhammad al-Karmi, may his blessings continue, said in investigating the topic, in these words): His statement: 'Ma huwa is a question about the complete essence': 'Ma huwa' is used once to explain the name of the thing asked about, and one asks with it about what underlies the verbal expression in terms of a nominal concept. So one says: 'What is grape?' and the answer is 'It is a fruit of fruits.' It is used a second time in the question about the complete essence of a thing. If the question restricts itself to mentioning a single thing, like our saying 'What is Zayd?', then the question is about the complete essence specific to him, so the species occurs in the answer: 'Man', for man is the complete essence specific to Zayd. This is when the single thing is a particular. If the single thing mentioned in the question is a universal reality, like 'What is man?', then the answer is the complete definition: 'Rational animal'. If the question combines multiple things, then the question is about the complete shared essence among those things. Then those multiple things, if they agree in reality, like Zayd, Amr, Bakr, and Khalid, then what is asked about is the complete agreed-upon unified reality among those things, so the species occurs in the answer as well. If they differ in reality, then what is asked about is the complete shared reality among those differing realities, and you have known that the complete shared essential among differing realities is the genus, so the genus occurs in the answer. Thus the genus must occur in answer to...
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the meaning of the question 'ma huwa' (what is it) as used in logic. It begins by distinguishing two types of 'ma': (1) 'ma al-shariha' (explanatory ma), which asks for the meaning of a name (e.g., 'What is grape?' answered by a nominal definition like 'a fruit'), and (2) 'ma al-haqiqa' (essential ma), which asks for the true quiddity. The commentator notes that in the definitions of genus and species, the 'ma' in 'ma huwa' is specifically 'ma al-haqiqa', not the explanatory one. If it were the explanatory, then accidental properties could be given in answer, which would wrongly include accidents under genus and species. Then the text provides a more elaborate analysis by Sheikh Muhammad al-Karmi, explaining how the answer to 'ma huwa' varies: if the question is about a particular like Zayd, the answer is the species (man); if about a universal like 'man', the answer is the complete definition (rational animal); if about multiple things that agree in reality (like Zayd, Amr, etc.), the answer is again the species; if about multiple things that differ in reality, the answer is the genus. This shows how genus and species are determined by the type of question and the agreement/difference of the realities. The passage ends abruptly but the logic is clear: the 'ma' in 'ma huwa' in the definitions of genus and species must be 'ma al-haqiqa'.

The Two Types of 'Ma' and Defining Genus and Species
Commentary on the two meanings of 'ma' and how they affect definitions of genus and species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the explicative and essential 'ma' and apply them correctly to evaluate definitions of genus and species in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
The type of 'ma' determines whether the answer is a definition or a description.

In Islamic logic, the interrogative 'ma' (what) is not a simple word. It carries two distinct meanings: one that asks for a mere explanation of a name, and another that asks for the true quiddity. This lesson unpacks this critical distinction and shows how it shapes the definitions of genus and species. Understanding this distinction prevents accidental properties from being mistaken for essential ones.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of 'ma'
Find in the passage the two types: the explicative 'ma' (ما الشارحة) used for explaining names, and the essential 'ma' (ما الحقيقة) used for asking about the complete reality. Note their distinct functions.
The essential 'ma' is also called 'ma al-haqiqa'.
2
Understand the consequences for 'ma huwa' questions
When the essential 'ma' is used in a 'ma huwa' question, the answer must be a genus or species (or a complete definition). When the explicative 'ma' is used, accidental properties can be given as answers. Trace this in the passage.
Accidental properties would invalidate definitions if mistakenly taken as genus or species.
3
Apply the distinction to the standard definitions
The passage critiques the definitions: genus as 'what is predicated of many things different in reality in answer to ma huwa' and species similarly. Check why these definitions fail unless 'ma' is restricted to the essential type.
Without restriction, the definitions would include accidental predicates, making them too broad.
4
Analyze the commentator's solution
Al-Muhashi proposes that logicians have specialized the word 'ma' to mean only the essential type. Evaluate this claim: is it a valid convention or does it create ambiguity?
This is a key move to preserve the definitions' precision.

(میرزا محمد علی (ره) وقال استاذنا الاعظم الشيخ محمد الكرمى دامت بركاته في تحقيق المقام ما هذا لفظه): قوله «ما هو سؤال عن تمام الحقيقة»: ما هو تستعمل مرة في شرح اسم المسؤل عنه ويسأل بها عما تحت «ماهو اللفظ من مفهوم اسمى فيقال: ما العنب ؟ فيجاب بانه فاكهة من الفواكه، وتستعمل ثانية في السؤال عن تمام حقيقة الشيء فان اقتصر فى السؤال على ذكر امر واحد مثل قولنا ما زيد؟» كان السؤال عن تمام الماهية المختصة به، فيقع النوع فى الجواب: (انسان) فان الانسان تمام ماهية زيد المختصة به، هذا اذا كان الامر الواحد امراً شخصياً، وان كان الامر الواحد المذكور فى السؤال حقيقة كلية نحو: ما الانسان؟ وقع فى الجواب الحد التام: حيوان ناطق)، و ان جمع في السؤال بين امور ،متعددة، كان السؤال عن تمام الماهية المشتركة بين تلك الأمور، ثم تلك الامور المتعددة ان كانت متفقة الحقيقة مثل زيد وعمرو و بكر و خاند، كان المسئول عنه تمام الحقيقة المتفقة المتحدة في تلك الامور فيقع النوع ايضاً في الجواب وان كانت مختلفة الحقيقة كان المسئول عنه تمام الحقيقة المشتركة بين تلك الحقائق المختلفة، وقد عرفت ان التمام الذاتي المشترك بين الحقائق المختلفة هو الجنس، فيقع الجنس في الجواب، فالجنس لابد ان يقع جواباً عن الماهية اى ماهية تفرض و عن الحقائق المخالفة للماهية المذكورة معها في السؤال المشاركة اياها في الجنس حتى يعرف ان الواقع فى الجواب، جنس لانوع ، لان السؤال عن الأمور المختلفة الحقايق المشتركة في ذاتي يعمها لا يجوز في جوابه الا الجنس لانّ ما تكفله السؤال من خصائصه وحده. فقول الشارح: المشاركة اياها في ذلك الجنس»، لا معنى له لانه لم يعهد جنس مذكور حتى يشار اليه، و الجنس المذكور في قوله «فالجنس لابد ان يقع جوابا المراد به الجنس من حيث هو جنس، لاجنس خاص حتى يعود اليه اسم الاشارة (التقريب ص (۷۰) قوله فيقع النوع ايضاً فى الجواب: لا يخفى انه ليس فيه دلالة على ان الحد التام لا يقع جواباً للسؤال عن الامور المتفقة الحقيقة حتى يقال: ان ما ذكر سابقاً من انه لاب. في رسوم الكليات من أحد الأمرين اما من تقدير لفظ الكلى فى الكلام واما من تقييد المقول بالافراد والايلزم عدم طرد التعريف غير وارد لعدم شمول المقول على الكثرة على حدود الكليات كما يظهر من المحشى ايضاً بل صرح بعض المحققين بان الحد التام يقع في جواب السؤال عن الامور المتفقة الحقيقة كما يقع النوع (ميرزا محمد على) (۷۱) ای الامور المجتمعة مختلفة الحقيقة كان المسؤل عنه بما هما او بما هم اذ المراد بالامور هو ما فوق الواحد. فان قلت : الجنس لا يقع الا في جواب ما هو. قلت : المراد تعيين ان الجنس لا يقال في جواب اى شيء. (عبدالرحیم) (۷۲) لا يخفى ان كون الكلى مقولاً فى جواب ما هو بحسب الشركة غير كونه جزء الماهية لانه انما يقال في جواب ماهو اذاسئل عن الماهية وغيرها معاً فيدل به على كمال حقيقتها من حيث وقع السؤال عن جملتها والمطلوب حكنه الحقيقة التى لها بالشركة وهو اعنى الكلى بهذا الاعتبار ليس بجزئى بل جنس وانما يقال الجزئى من حيث انه يتركب منه و من غيره الماهية و هذا الاعتبار غير حاصل الاعتبار الاول فاذن مفهوم كون الشىء جنساً مغاير لمفهوم كونه جزء و ان كان معروضها ذاتا واحدة فلايردان الجنس جزء ممامر والجزء لا يحمل فاحفظ ذلك (عبد الرحيم) (۷۳) قوله فان كان اى الجنس مع كونه جواباً عن الماهية وعن بعض الحقائق المخالفة لها المشاركة اياها في الجنس جواباً عن الماهية وعن كل واحدة من الماهيات المختلفة المشاركة لها في ذلك الجنس، فالجنس ،قريب كالحيوان حيث يقع جواباً عن الانسان و عن كل ما يشاركه في الماهية الحيوانية و ان لم يقع الجنس جواباً عن الماهية اى ماهية تفرض وتذكر في السؤال و عن كل ما يشاركها في الجنس الابواسطة او وسائط، فيعيد كالجسم حيث يجاب به عن السؤال ما هو الانسان و الفرس والحمار فانه انما يقع فى الجواب بواسطة الجسم النامي ومن بعده بواسطة الحيوان. واذا احتوى السؤال على ماهيات مختلفة الحقائق ولكنها تشترك فى ذاتى بعيد عن بعضها وقريب للبعض الآخر، فان وقع هذا الذاتى المشترك فى الجواب، فهو بالنسبة الى ما هو بعيد منه، بعيد، وبالنسبة الى ما هو قريب منه ،قريب مثلا اذا سأل: ما هو الانسان و الشجر والحجر؟ فلابد ان يقع في الجواب ما يراعى به جال الكل و هو الجسم المطلق، فالجسم المطلق جنس قريب بالنسبة الى الحجر و بعيد عن الانسان والشجر عن الانسان بواسطتين الجسم النامي والحيوان وعن الشجر بواسطة هو الجسم النامي
(69) His statement 'ma huwa is a question about the complete reality' — because the word 'ma' is of two types: the explicative 'ma' which is used to ask for explanation of the name and the exposition of its concept and what meaning it is placed for, and the essential 'ma' which is used to ask for the true quiddity. So when one asks about things that are consistent in reality or different in reality using the essential 'ma', the species or genus occurs in the answer. When one asks about them using the explicative 'ma', it is permissible that accidental properties occur in the answer, as the author stated in Sharh al-Talkhis. So it is not valid to define genus as 'that which is predicated of many things different in reality in answer to 'ma huwa', and to define species as 'that which is predicated of many things consistent in reality in answer to 'ma huwa', for otherwise accidental properties would be included under genus and species by the truth of the definition applying to them. The commentator (al-Muhashi) indicated that by the word 'ma' only the essential 'ma' is meant, not absolute 'ma', so that what was mentioned may not be objected. And it is not hidden that this would entail using a homonymous word in the definition without a specifying context, unless it is claimed that the word 'ma', although in the original language it is used for both meanings, has become specialized in the terminology of the people of the scale (logicians) to the essential 'ma'. (Mirza Muhammad Ali (may Allah have mercy on him) said, and our greatest master, Shaykh Muhammad al-Karmi (may his blessings endure), said in investigation of the context, verbatim:) His statement 'ma huwa is a question about the complete reality': 'ma huwa' is used once in explaining the name of the thing asked about, and one asks by it about what the word (the expression) is of a nominal concept — so one says: 'What is grapes?' and the answer is 'it is a fruit of fruits.' And it is used a second time in asking about the complete reality of the thing. If the question is limited to mentioning a single thing, such as our saying 'What is Zayd?', then the question is about the complete quiddity specific to him, and the species occurs in the answer: 'human', for the human is the complete quiddity specific to Zayd. This applies when the single thing is a particular thing. But if the single thing mentioned in the question is a universal reality, like 'What is man?', then the complete definition occurs in the answer: 'rational animal'. And if the question combines multiple things, then the question is about the shared complete quiddity among those things. Then if those multiple things are consistent in reality, like Zayd, 'Amr, Bakr, and Khalid, then what is asked about is the complete reality that is consistent and unified in those things, so species also occurs in the answer. And if they are different in reality, then what is asked about is the complete reality shared among those different realities, and you have known that the complete essential shared among different realities is the genus, so the genus occurs in the answer. So the genus must occur as an answer concerning the quiddity — i.e., any quiddity that is supposed — and concerning the realities that are different from the mentioned quiddity that accompany it in the question, sharing in that genus, so that it is known that what occurs in the answer is a genus, not a species, because the question about things different in reality that share an essential that encompasses them — only the genus is permissible in answer, because what the question encompasses of its properties alone. So the statement of the commentator (al-Sharh) 'sharing with it in that genus' has no meaning, because no genus has been previously mentioned to which the demonstrative pronoun 'that' would refer. And the genus mentioned in his saying 'so the genus must occur as an answer' — what is meant is the genus insofar as it is a genus, not a particular genus to which the demonstrative pronoun would return. (Taqrib, page (70)) His saying 'so species also occurs in the answer': it is not hidden that this does not indicate that the complete definition does not occur as an answer to the question about things consistent in reality, such that it could be said that what was mentioned previously — that it is necessary in the definitions of the universals that either the word 'universal' is implied in the speech or the predicate is restricted to individuals, else the definition would not be comprehensive — is not applicable because the predicate 'predicated of many' does not apply to the definitions of the universals, as also appears from the commentator (al-Muhashi). Rather, some investigators have stated that the complete definition occurs as an answer to the question about things consistent in reality just as the species occurs. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (71) i.e., the gathered things, different in reality, what is asked about is 'what are they?' or 'who are they?', because by 'things' is meant what is above one. If you say: 'The genus only occurs in answer to 'ma huwa'. I say: The intent is to specify that the genus is not said in answer to any other thing. (Abd al-Rahim) (72) It is not hidden that the universal being predicated in answer to 'ma huwa' in respect of commonality is not the same as it being a part of the quiddity. Because it is said in answer to 'ma huwa' when one asks about the quiddity and other things together, so it indicates the completeness of its reality from the perspective that the question was about the totality, and the required is the ruling of the reality that belongs to it by commonality. And it — the universal in this regard — is not particular but genus. The particular is only said in that it is composed from it and from other than it the quiddity. And this regard is not obtained in the first regard. So the concept of something being a genus is different from its concept of being a part, even if their substrates are one essence. So do not let the two be confused: genus as part and the part does not predicate. So preserve that. (Abd al-Rahim) (73) His saying 'for if [the genus] is [an answer]' — i.e., the genus, while being an answer concerning the quiddity and concerning some of the realities that differ from it and share with it in the genus, is an answer concerning the quiddity and concerning each one of the different quiddities that share with it in that genus. So the proximate genus like 'animal' occurs as an answer about man and about everything that shares with it in animality. And if the genus does not occur as an answer concerning the quiddity (i.e., any quiddity supposed and mentioned in the question) and concerning everything that shares with it in the genus except by means of intermediaries, then it is higher, like 'body', by which one answers the question 'what is man, horse, and donkey?' for it only occurs in answer through the intermediary of 'growing body' and then through 'animal'. And if the question contains quiddities different in reality but they share in an essential that is distant to some and proximate to others, then if this shared essential occurs in the answer, it is distant relative to what is distant from it and proximate relative to what is proximate. For example, if one asks: 'What are man, tree, and stone?', then the answer must be what takes care of the entire group, and it is 'absolute body'. So absolute body is a proximate genus relative to stone and distant from man and tree: from man by two intermediaries (growing body and animal) and from tree by one intermediary (growing body).
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the two types of the interrogative 'ma' (ما) and how they relate to the definition of genus and species. The author first distinguishes the explicative 'ma' (ما الشارحة), used to ask for the explanation of a name or concept, from the essential 'ma' (ما الحقيقة), used to ask for the complete quiddity. When asked with the essential 'ma', the answer can be a species (if the things asked about are consistent in reality) or a genus (if they are different). If asked with the explicative 'ma', accidental properties can be given as answers. The commentator then critiques a common definition of genus and species because it would include accidental properties if the 'ma' is not restricted to the essential type. Al-Muhashi suggests that the 'ma' in those definitions should be taken exclusively as the essential 'ma', even though it is ambiguous in language, because logicians have specialized it. The passage then provides a detailed explanation by Shaykh Muhammad al-Karmi on how 'ma huwa' is used: for a single particular thing (like Zayd), the answer is the species (human); for a single universal (like man), the answer is the complete definition (rational animal); for multiple things consistent in reality, the answer is the species; for multiple things different in reality, the answer is the genus. The genus must be the answer that covers all the different realities. The commentary also addresses the distinction between genus as a part of the quiddity and genus as a universal predicated in answer to 'ma huwa', emphasizing that being a part is not the same as being a predicate. Finally, it explains how proximate and distant genera function: a proximate genus (like animal for man) requires no intermediary, while a distant genus (like body for man) requires a chain of intermediaries. Examples from animals and minerals illustrate these points.

Key Terms
ما الشارحة
ما الحقيقة
الجنس
النوع
الحد التام

Checkpoint

A) Because the explicative 'ma' asks for a definition, not a name.
B) Because if the explicative 'ma' were allowed, accidental predicates could also be answers, making the definitions too broad.
C) Because genus and species are only defined by the essential 'ma' in Arabic grammar.
D) Because the explicative 'ma' is not used in logic.

Key Takeaway
The two types of 'ma' are crucial: the essential 'ma' yields genus/species/definitions, while the explicative 'ma' yields accidental descriptions; definitions of genus and species must be based on the essential 'ma' alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Consider the question 'What is ghusl?' (ritual bath). Using the essential 'ma', what should the answer be? Should it be a genus like 'cleansing', a species like 'purification', or a complete definition? Explain using the distinction from the passage.
?
