External and Intellectual Parts: Distinguishing Conceptual from Concrete
Defines juz' khariji and juz' 'aqli with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between an external part (juz' khariji) and an intellectual part (juz' 'aqli) in a given definition, and explain why each type matters for analyzing conceptual wholes in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
The external part is separable; the intellectual part is inseparable in existence but essential in definition.

In Islamic logic, when we analyze the definition of a thing, we must determine whether its parts are external (physically distinct) or intellectual (conceptually distinct). This lesson defines these two types using the examples of a hand as an external part of the body, and 'animal' as an intellectual part of 'man'. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating the completeness of definitions in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the whole being analyzed
The passage discusses parts of a whole. Examples: hand is part of body; animal is part of man.
2
Recognize the criterion for external part
An external part has a distinct existence separate from the whole. Example: a hand can exist apart from the body (mentally or physically).
3
Recognize the criterion for intellectual part
An intellectual part does not have a distinct existence; it exists only as a concept within the whole. Example: 'animal' as part of 'man' cannot be separated from man in reality.
4
Test the distinction with a new example
Given a definition like 'human is a rational animal', identify which part is intellectual (animal) and which might be external? (None; both are intellectual, but rational is a differentia).
5
Apply to the simple species note
The passage mentions that even a simple point can have an intellectual part (its genus). This shows that the intellectual part is about conceptual analysis, not physical composition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ان يكون للنقطة جزء عقلى هو جنس لها و ان لم يكن لها جزء فى الخارج وعلى كل حال فامكان النوع البسيط كاف فى ان يكون فارقاً بين النوع الحقيقى والاضافي. والتوسع فى ان الأمور العقلية كيف تكون اجزاء في الامور المادية وهل يكفي في اطلاق الجزئية عليها للأمور المادية اتصالها بها اتصال [؟] تدبير ليس من مباحث هذا الكتاب والاكتفاء بما أجملناه آنفاً (التقريب ص ٣٢٣١) (۸۳) الجزء الخارجى هو الذى يكون داخلاً في الكل و يكون وجوده متميزاً عن وجوده كاليد مثلا والجزء العقلى هو الذى يكون داخلا فى الكل ويكون وجوده غير متميز عن وجوده كالحيوان مثلاً فانه جزء الانسان وليس وجوده متميزاً عن وجوده، فتأمل . (محمد على)
That the point has an intellectual part which is its genus even if it has no part externally. In any case, the possibility of a simple species is sufficient to be a differentia between the real species and the relative species. Elaboration on how intellectual things can be parts of material things and whether it suffices to label them as parts of material things due to their connection through [؟] administration is not among the discussions of this book, and sufficing with what we summarized earlier (Taqrib p. 3231). (83) The external part is that which is internal to the whole and its existence is distinct from the existence of the whole, like a hand for example. The intellectual part is that which is internal to the whole and its existence is not distinct from the existence of the whole, like animal for example, for it is a part of man and its existence is not distinct from his existence. Reflect. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage, from the glosses on the hashia, defines two types of parts: the external part (juz' khariji) and the intellectual part (juz' 'aqli). The external part has a distinct existence from the whole, like a hand which is a part of the body and can be considered separately. The intellectual part does not have a distinct existence; it is a conceptual part like 'animal' in 'man'—you cannot separate animality from man in external existence. The passage begins by stating that even a simple point can have an intellectual part as its genus, even if it has no external parts. The possibility of a simple species is enough to differentiate between real and relative species. The commentator then notes that delving into how intellectual things become parts of material things is beyond the scope of this book. This is a standard logical distinction used to analyze definitions.

Key Terms
الجزء الخارجي
الجزء العقلي
النوع البسيط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
An external part has separate existence; an intellectual part exists only conceptually but is essential to the definition.

Why Genera and Species Chains Must End
Proves that ascending in genera and descending in species cannot be infinite

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to demonstrate why the chains of genera (ascending) and species (descending) must terminate, using the two arguments based on inconceivability of infinite parts and necessity of individuals.

Core Idea
Infinite regress in definitions is impossible: it makes essences inconceivable and individuals unreal.

This passage from a logical commentary establishes a foundational principle: the hierarchy of genera and species cannot go on forever. The argument is that if the ascent were infinite, the quiddity (essence) would consist of infinite parts, which the finite intellect cannot grasp — yet we clearly conceive quiddities. Similarly, if the descent of species had no end, no individuals could exist, which contradicts our certain knowledge that individuals exist. Understanding this ensures that definitions can be fully complete, with a highest genus and a final species.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two claims
The passage makes two separate arguments: (1) the ascent from particular to universal genera must end at a genus above which there is none; (2) the descent in the species chain must end at a species below which there is none. Find these two statements in the Arabic.
Look for 'الترقى' (ascent) and 'التنزل' (descent).
2
Analyze the ascent argument
The proof says: if the ascent were infinite, the quiddity would be composed of infinite parts. Conceptualizing such a quiddity would require encompassing the infinite, which is impossible for the human intellect. Yet we do conceive quiddities spontaneously — so the ascent cannot be infinite.
The reasoning is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Analyze the descent argument
The proof says: if the descent were infinite, there would be no lowest species. Without a lowest species, no individuals could exist (since individuals are instances of the final species). But we know individuals certainly exist — so the descent must terminate.
The argument relies on the necessary link between species and individuals.
4
Connect to definition theory
These two conditions ensure that every essence can be defined by a finite set of differentiae ending at a lowest species, and that all definitions share a highest genus. Without this, the entire project of definition (hadd) would be impossible.
In Islamic logic, definitions must be complete and finite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله بان يكون الترقى من خاص الى عام لا يخفى انه لا يجوز ان يذهب هذا الترقى الى غير النهاية بل لابد وان ينتهى إلى جنس لاجنس له فوقه لأن تركب الماهية من الاجزاء الغير المتناهية غير معقول لاستلزام تصورها ح احاطة العقل بالامور الغير المتناهية و هو محال و مستلزم المحال محال مع انا نتصور الماهية بالبداهة، وكذا لا يجوز ان يذهب التنزل فى سلسلة الانواع الى غير النهاية بل لابد ان ينتهى الى نوع لا يكون تحته نوع والايلزم ان لا يتحقق الاشخاص وهو باطل اما الملازمة، فلان تحققها يستلزم تحقق الانواع وهو خلاف الفرض واما البطلان فلضرورة تحقق الاشخاص. (میرزا محمد علی)
He said: that the ascent from particular to universal is not hidden; it is not permissible for this ascent to go to infinity, rather it must end at a genus above which there is no genus, because the composition of quiddity from infinite parts is inconceivable since its conceptualization would require the intellect's encompassing of infinite things, which is impossible, and what entails impossibility is impossible, especially since we conceive quiddity spontaneously. Likewise, it is not permissible for the descent in the chain of species to go to infinity, rather it must end at a species below which there is no species; otherwise individuals would not be realized, which is false. As for the entailment, because their realization entails the realization of the species, contrary to the hypothesis. As for the falsehood, it is due to the necessity of the realization of individuals.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that the hierarchy of genera must have a highest genus and the hierarchy of species a lowest species. For genera: if the ascent were infinite, the quiddity (essence) would consist of infinite parts, which is impossible because the human intellect cannot fully comprehend an infinity, yet we clearly conceive quiddities. For species: if the descent were infinite, there would be no final species, and then no individuals could exist, which is false because individuals do exist. The proof uses a reductio ad absurdum and is foundational for the theory of definition, ensuring that definitions can terminate at a highest genus and final differentia.

Key Terms
الترقى
التنزل
الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why must the chain of genera terminate?
A) Because all genera are observable in nature
B) Because otherwise quiddity would have infinite parts, making it inconceivable
C) Because logicians have decreed it
D) Because species are finite in number

Key Takeaway
The hierarchy of universals must be finite to allow conceptualization and the existence of individuals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Order of Transition: From Specific to General in Genera
Explains why genera are ordered from specific to general and species from general to specific

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the epistemological reason for ordering genera from specific to general and species from general to specific.

Core Idea
We define from known to unknown: specific before general for genera.

When defining a genus, should we start with the most general or the most specific? This passage explains that human cognition grasps the specific more readily, so we begin with the specific and ascend to the general. For species, we start with the general and descend. Understanding this order is essential for constructing proper definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two directions of transition
Notice that there are two distinct orders: one for genera (moving from specific to general) and one for species (moving from general to specific). The passage provides separate explanations for each.
2
Understand the epistemological reason for genera
The text says 'man does not grasp the encompassment of the more general except through the more specific.' This means the specific is better known to us, so we start with it.
The example: growing body (specific) then animal (more general) then absolute body, then substance.
3
Understand the epistemological reason for species
The text says 'the species of a species is a portion of the species, and the portion is only clarified by that from which the portion is derived.' So we need the general to understand the specific.
Example: absolute body (general) then growing body (specific), then animal, then man.
4
Apply to the hierarchy of natural kinds
The examples given (substance → absolute body → growing body → animal → man) illustrate a taxonomic chain. Practice tracing the order in both directions.
This hierarchy is borrowed from Aristotelian natural philosophy, but the method applies to any classification system.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله وذلك اى لم يكون الانتقال في الاجناس من خاص الى عام؟ لان جنس الجنس جزء منه مقول عليه و على غيره من الاجناس المشاركة له فهو اعم منه والانسان لا تتضح له الاحاطة بالاعم الامن الاخص فيبدأ بالاخص ثم بالاعم منه و هكذا (التقريب ص ٣٢) كالجسم النامي الذي هو جنس للحيوان فانه اعم من الحيوان و كذا الجسم المطلق بالنسبة الى الجسم النامي والجوهر بالنسبة الى الجسم المطلق، والسر في ذلك ان جنسية الشيء انما تكون بالنسبة الى ما تحته كما ان نوعية الشيء انما تكون بالنسبة الى ما فوقه . (محمد على) قوله وذلك: أى لم يكون الانتقال فى الانواع من الاعم الى الاخص؟ لان نوع النوع حصة من النوع والحصة انما تتضح بما منه الحصة فلذا يبدأ بالاعم من الانواع ثم منه الى الاخص منه و هكذا. (التقريب ص ٣٢) كالجسم النامى الذى هو نوع الجسم المطلق فانه اخص من الجسم المطلق و كذا الحيوان بالنسبة الى الجسم النامي والانسان بالنسبة الى الحيوان (محمد على)
He said: 'That is, why is the transition in genera from specific to general?' Because the genus of a genus is part of it, predicated on it and on other genera sharing with it, so it is more general than it. And man does not grasp the encompassment of the more general except through the more specific. So begins with the specific, then the more general, and so on. (Taqrib p. 32) Example: the growing body which is a genus for animal, for it is more general than animal; likewise absolute body relative to growing body, and substance relative to absolute body. The secret is that the genus-ness of a thing is only in relation to what is under it, just as the species-ness of a thing is only in relation to what is above it. (Muhammad Ali) He said: 'That is, why is the transition in species from general to specific?' Because the species of a species is a portion of the species, and the portion is only clarified by that from which the portion is derived. So one begins with the more general of the species, then from it to the more specific, and so on. (Taqrib p. 32) Example: the growing body which is a species of absolute body, for it is more specific than absolute body; likewise animal relative to growing body, and man relative to animal. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage explains the pedagogical order for defining genera and species. For genera, we move from the more specific (e.g., animal) to the more general (e.g., growing body, absolute body, substance) because the specific is more directly known to the human mind. For species, we move from the more general (e.g., substance) to the more specific (e.g., man) because the species is a portion of the genus and is understood by reference to the genus. The examples illustrate a concrete hierarchy in natural philosophy: substance → absolute body → growing body → animal → man. The 'secret' mentioned is that something is a genus only in relation to what is below it, and a species only in relation to what is above it.

Key Terms
الانتقال (al-intiqal): The mental transition or order of investigation from one concept to another. [Note: No significant shift.]
جنس الجنس (jins al-jins): The genus of a genus; the higher genus that subsumes the immediate genus. [Note: No significant shift.]
نوع النوع (naw' al-naw'): The species of a species; the lower species subsumed under the immediate species. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because it is better known to us
C) Because it is the highest in the chain
D) Because it is the ultimate goal

Key Takeaway
The order of definition follows epistemological accessibility: specific before general for genera, and general before specific for species.

Why the Answer Must Be Only the Differentia
Commentary confirming that the answer must be solely the differentia when the genus is known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the answer to a definitional question must be solely the differentia when the genus is known, and identify the reasoning from the commentary.

Core Idea
Only the differentia adds new knowledge; the genus is already known.

In the logical tradition, when someone asks 'what is it in itself?' about a species, the genus is assumed known. This commentary explains why the answer must be only the differentia, not the genus or a composite. Let's examine the reasoning behind this rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the question type
The definitional question 'what is it in itself?' (ma huwa bi-dhatihi) assumes the questioner already knows the genus. Here, the genus 'animal' is known.
2
Extract the commentator's rule
The commentator states that the answer must be only the differentia, in this case 'rational' (natiq). Neither the genus alone nor the composite is permitted.
3
Understand why the genus is excluded
Because the genus is known to the questioner, repeating it would be 'repletion of what has been obtained' – adding no new knowledge.
4
Recognize the composite case
Answering with 'rational animal' includes the known genus, so it also fails to provide new knowledge. Only the differentia is informative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۰۹) قوله فتعين الجواب بأنه ناطق لا غير أي لا يجوز أن يجاب بأنه حيوان ناطق أو حيوان لأن الحيوان كان معلوماً للسائل فلا يجوز أن يقع في الجواب مجرداً أو منضماً إلى الفصل، لأن ذلك تحصيل الحاصل أو ارتكاب بما لا فائدة فيه. (محمد علي) (۱۱۰) أي بأسره وتمامه أي إشكال وقوع الحد في جواب أي شيء وصدق التعريف على الحد والجنس. ثم لا وجه لتخصيص دفع الإشكال بحذافيره بهذا الجواب كما هو ظاهر كلامه فإنه بالجواب الأول أيضاً يندفع الإشكال بحذافيره كما بينا آنفاً. ويمكن أن يكون قوله: «فحينئذ» إشارة إلى جميع ما تقدم من كلام صاحب المحاكمات وكلام المحقق الطوسي أو يكون قوله «فحينئذ» إلى آخره من كلام المحقق الطوسي (ره) لا من كلام المحشي أو أن يكون توهم المحشي في جواب صاحب المحاكمات أنه إنما يندفع به اللزوم الثاني فقط لا الأول كما توهمه بعض المحققين من المحشين وقد مر. (الشيخ محمد علي)
(109) His statement: 'So the answer is determined as 'rational' only' means that it is not permissible to answer with 'rational animal' or 'animal', because 'animal' was known to the questioner, so it is not permissible for it to occur in the answer alone or joined with the differentia, because that is a repletion of what has been obtained or engaging in something useless. (Muhammad Ali) (110) That is, entirely and completely: i.e., the problem of the occurrence of the definition in answer to 'what is it' and the truth of the definition on the definition and the genus. Then there is no basis for specifying the complete repulsion of the problem to this answer, as is apparent from his speech, for by the first answer also the problem is repelled completely, as we explained earlier. And it is possible that his statement 'then' refers to all that preceded from the speech of the author of al-Muhakamat and the speech of al-Muhaqqiq al-Tusi, or that his statement 'then' etc. is from the speech of al-Muhaqqiq al-Tusi (r.a.), not from the speech of the commentator, or that the commentator's misunderstanding in answering the author of al-Muhakamat is that only the second implication is repelled by it, not the first, as some of the verifying commentators imagined, and it has passed. (Shaykh Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage consists of two marginal comments. The first (109) explains why the answer to a definitional question must be only the differentia (here 'rational') and not the genus ('animal') or the composite ('rational animal'). The reason is that the genus 'animal' was already known to the questioner; repeating it or including it with the differentia is useless (because it adds no new knowledge). The second comment (110) clarifies that this answer completely repels the problem of the definition occurring as a valid answer. However, the commentator notes that the first answer also repels the problem completely, so there is no need to limit the repulsion to only this answer. He discusses various possibilities regarding the source of the phrase 'then' in the text, suggesting it might be from al-Tusi rather than the commentator.

Key Takeaway
When the genus is known, the answer to a definitional question must be only the differentia; anything else is redundant.

The Muqawwim is the Differentia
States that the constitutive part is the differentia and part of reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the muqawwim (constitutive part) in a definition and explain why it is part of the essence.

Core Idea
The constitutive part of a species is its differentia, belonging to the reality.

In logic, definitions are composed of essential parts. This passage clarifies that the constitutive part (muqawwim) being discussed is precisely the differentia (fasl). The differentia is not an external accident but an integral component of the true reality (haqiqa) of the individuals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being clarified
The term 'muqawwim' (constitutive part) is the focus. Recognize that it is not a genus but a differentia.
2
Understand the equation: muqawwim = fasl
The passage states that the muqawwim is the differentia (fasl). This identifies the kind of constitutive part meant.
3
Recall the nature of the differentia
The differentia is a part of the reality (haqiqa) of its individuals. This means it is essential, not accidental.
4
Apply to definitions
In any complete definition (hadd), the differentia must be a constitutive part that distinguishes the species and belongs to its essence.

Source Text

(114) As for the minor [premise], because the muqawwim is the differentia, and it has preceded that the differentia is part of the reality of its individuals.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This short note explains a specific point, likely in a syllogistic context. It identifies the 'muqawwim' (constitutive part) as the differentia (fasl). It then restates the established principle that the differentia is a part of the true reality (haqiqa) of the individuals that fall under the species. This reinforces the idea that the differentia is not an accident but an essential constituent.

Answer to 'What is it?' Must Be Only the Differentia
Author explains why the answer to 'what is it?' must be the differentia alone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and avoid the fallacy of tahsil al-hasil in definitions by ensuring that the answer to 'what is it?' is only the differentia.

Core Idea
The differentia alone gives new knowledge; repeating the genus is a logical fallacy.

When someone asks 'what is it?' about a thing, they already know the genus. Therefore, including the genus in the answer is useless repetition. In this passage, the commentator explains why the answer must be just the differentia, using the example of defining a human as 'rational' (natiq) rather than 'rational animal'. This principle, called tahsil al-hasil, is crucial for constructing sound definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the question and the components
The question 'what is it?' assumes the questioner knows the genus. For instance, if asked 'what is a human?', the genus 'animal' is already known. The differentia 'rational' is what distinguishes humans.
In the passage, the commentator says the animal is already known to the questioner.
2
Recognize why the genus cannot be included
Including the genus in the answer would be 'tahsil al-hasil' (obtaining what is already obtained) – a logical fallacy because it adds no new information. The answer would be redundant.
The commentator explicitly calls this 'tahsil al-hasil' and 'something of no benefit'.
3
Apply the rule to a definition
When constructing a definition, answer only with the differentia. For human, answer 'rational' (natiq) alone. The composite 'rational animal' is not permissible because it repeats the known genus.
The commentator states: 'it is not permissible to answer with 'rational animal' or 'animal'.'
4
Test for the fallacy in other definitions
Given any definition, check if it includes terms already known to the questioner. If so, it commits tahsil al-hasil and must be reduced to only the new information – typically the differentia.
This principle applies in all Islamic sciences that use definitions (hadd).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۰۹) قوله فتعين الجواب بانه ناطق :لاغير اى لا يجوز ان يجاب بانه حیوان ناطق او حیوان لان الحيوان كان معلوماً للسائل فلا يجوز ان يقع فى الجواب مجرداً او منضماً إلى الفصل، لان ذلك تحصيل الحاصل او ارتكاب بما لا فاائدة فيه. (محمد على)
(109) His statement: 'So the answer is determined: that he is rational, not otherwise.' Meaning: it is not permissible to answer with 'he is a rational animal' or 'animal', because the animal is already known to the questioner. So it is not permissible for it to occur in the answer alone or joined with the differentia, because that would be obtaining the already obtained (tahsil al-hasil) or engaging in something of no benefit. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage explains a point in the main text (shawh) about the correct answer when asked 'what is it?' (ayy shay). The commentator, Muhammad Ali, expands on why the answer must be only the differentia (natiq) and not include the genus (hayawan) or the composite (hayawan natiq). The reason is that the genus is already known to the questioner (the genus is presupposed). Including it would be 'tahsil al-hasil' (obtaining what is already obtained) or useless repetition. The differentia alone provides new information essential to the definition.

Key Terms
ناطق
تحصيل الحاصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The answer to 'what is it?' must be the differentia alone to avoid the fallacy of tahsil al-hasil (obtaining the already obtained).

Distinguishing Necessary of Essence and Necessary of Existence
Critical refinement on the definition and division of necessary properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between lāzim al-māhiyya and lāzim al-wujūd and explain why the commentator used 'subject' instead of 'essence' in the division of necessary properties.

Core Idea
Some necessary properties belong to the essence itself; others only when it exists.

This passage refines a technical point in Islamic logic: how we divide necessary properties (lāzim). A commentator initially avoided saying 'essence' to prevent a potential objection. A later scholar shows that even if 'essence' were used, the objection fails. The key is understanding that 'essence' can refer to the essence in itself or the existing essence, leading to two distinct types of necessary properties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the commentator's precaution
Notice that the commentator said 'concerning its subject' (عَنْ مَعْرُوضِهِ) instead of 'concerning the essence' (عَنِ المَاهِيَةِ). This was deliberate to avoid an apparent objection about the division of necessary properties.
The objection is that the division into necessary of essence and necessary of existence would then present the latter as separable from essence.
2
Understand the objection
The imagined objection: if the division were of the necessary of essence, then 'necessary of existence' would be a part that is not impossible to separate from essence, contradicting the definition of necessity.
The objector assumes that 'necessary of existence' can be separated from essence, at least in thought.
3
Analyze the scholar's response
The responding scholar argues that even with 'essence', the objection fails. He claims that 'necessary of existence' is not inseparable from the essence as such, but it is inseparable from the essence when it exists. The term 'essence' in the division can refer to the existing essence.
The key move: 'essence' in this context means 'existing essence', so the division holds because both types are inseparable from the existing essence in general.
4
Grasp the final distinction
The passage concludes that the objection does not refute the commentator. The distinction between lāzim al-māhiyya (inseparable from essence as such) and lāzim al-wujūd (inseparable from essence when it exists) is maintained when 'essence' is taken as 'existing essence'.
The word 'thing' (شَيْء) in the division covers both types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال بعض المحققين من المحشين: انما قال عن معروضه ولم يقل عن الماهية كما قاله ،بعضهم لثلا يرد عليه ظاهراً ان التقسيم الذى يذكره لللازم، تقسيم الشيء الى نفسه و هو لازم الماهية و الى غيره وهو لازم الوجود فانه مما لا يستحيل انفكاكه عن الماهية وبالنظر الى هذا اخذ مورد القسمة فيما بعد لازم الشيء لا لازم الماهية هذا كلامه رفع مقامه. عن الماهية مطلقا بل :اقول انما قال: «ظاهراً لان ايراد المذكور لا يرده في الحقيقة على تقدير ان يقول عن الماهية ويأخذ مورد القسمة لازم الماهية ايضاً، فانا لا نسلم ان لازم الوجود مما لا يستحيل انفكاكه من حيث هى هى ولا يلزم منه أن لا يستحيل انفكاكه منها من حيث الوجود أيضاً. يمتنع والحاصل: انا نعنى باللازم الذي هو مورد القسمة ما. انفکاکه عن الا الماهية في الجملة لا من حيث هي هي والايراد انما يأتي على ذلك التقدير دون الاول فان امتناع الانفكاك في الجملة يصدق بامتناعه من حيث هى هى وبامتناعه من حيث الوجود. ولو سلم فنقول : المراد بالماهية الماهية الموجودة فما يمتنع انفكاكه عنها اما ان يكون ممتنع الانفكاك من حيث هي هي اولا، الاول لازم الماهية و الثاني لازم الوجود. ثم التحقيق ان الايراد المذكور لا يرد على المحشى على الظاهر ايضاً و ان يدل لفظ الشيء و المعروض
One of the scholar commentators said: He only said 'concerning its subject' and did not say 'concerning the essence' as some said, lest it might be objected apparently that the division he mentions for the necessary property is a division of the thing into itself, which is the necessary of the essence, and into other, which is the necessary of existence, for it is something whose separation from the essence is not impossible. And in view of this, he took the subject of division afterwards as the necessary of the thing, not the necessary of the essence. This is his statement, may his rank be elevated. (But) I say: He only said 'apparently' because the mentioned objection does not refute him in reality on the assumption that he said 'concerning the essence' and took the subject of division as the necessary of the essence also. For we do not concede that the necessary of existence is something whose separation from it (the essence) is impossible from the perspective of itself (the essence), and it does not entail that its separation from it from the perspective of existence is also impossible. (It is) impossible. The conclusion: We mean by the necessary that is the subject of division what cannot be separated from the essence in general (fi al-jumla), not from the perspective of itself (min haythu hiya hiya). And the objection only arises on that assumption, not the first. For the impossibility of separation in general is true both for impossibility from the perspective of itself and from the perspective of existence. And if it is conceded, we say: What is meant by essence is the existing essence. So what cannot be separated from it is either that whose separation is impossible from the perspective of itself or not. The first is the necessary of the essence, and the second is the necessary of existence. Then the verification is that the mentioned objection does not refute the commentator even apparently, and that the word 'thing' and 'subject' indicate...
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement on the division of necessary properties (لازم). The commentator initially stated that the division is of the necessary of the thing, not the necessary of the essence. A scholar suggests that this was to avoid the objection that the division would then be into the necessary of the essence and the necessary of existence, where the latter is not inseparable from the essence. The speaker then responds that even if the commentator had said 'concerning the essence', the objection would not refute him, because the necessary of existence is not inseparable from the essence as such but from the existing essence. The term 'necessary' in the division refers to what cannot be separated from the essence in general, which includes both necessary of essence and necessary of existence. Thus, the division is valid. The speaker concludes that the objection does not refute the commentator, as indicated by the use of the terms 'thing' and 'subject'.

Key Terms
لازم الماهية
لازم الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The first is logical, the second is physical
C) The first is universal, the second is particular
D) There is no difference

Key Takeaway
The division of necessary properties into those of essence and those of existence is valid when 'essence' is understood as 'existing essence'.

Why 'Subject' Not 'Essence': Precision in Dividing Necessary Concomitants
Defense of using 'subject' instead of 'essence' in dividing necessary concomitants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the logician uses 'subject' instead of 'essence' when dividing necessary concomitants, and identify the subtle distinction between lazimum of essence and lazimum of existence.

Core Idea
Precision in terminology avoids logical contradictions in dividing necessary concomitants.

In the study of necessary concomitants (lazim), logicians must carefully define the basis of division. In this passage, the commentator defends his choice to use 'subject' (ma'rud) instead of 'essence' (mahiyya), anticipating an objection that would make the division self-defeating. This lesson unpacks that terminological refinement and its logical motivation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
What problem arises if 'essence' is used instead of 'subject'? The objector claims that dividing the necessary concomitant into lazimum of essence and lazimum of existence would incorrectly split a thing into itself and something else.
2
Understand the Division
The two types: lazimum of essence (inseparable from the essence as such) and lazimum of existence (inseparable from the existence of the essence).
3
Follow the Response
The author responds that using 'subject' (or 'thing') avoids the objection because the division is not about the essence per se but about the necessary concomitant in a general sense, which encompasses both types without self-division.
4
Grasp the Nuance
The phrase 'in general' (fi al-jumla) covers impossibility of separation from the essence from any perspective, whether from the essence as such or from its existence. This subtlety preserves logical coherence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال بعض المحققين من المحشين: انما قال عن معروضه ولم يقل عن الماهية كما قاله بعضهم لئلا يرد عليه ظاهراً ان التقسيم الذى يذكره لللازم، تقسيم الشيء الى نفسه و هو لازم الماهية و الى غيره وهو لازم الوجود فانه مما لا يستحيل انفكاكه عن الماهية وبالنظر الى هذا اخذ مورد القسمة فيما بعد لازم الشيء لا لازم الماهية هذا كلامه رفع مقامه. اقول انما قال: «ظاهراً لان ايراد المذكور لا يرده في الحقيقة على تقدير ان يقول عن الماهية ويأخذ مورد القسمة لازم الماهية ايضاً، فانا لا نسلم ان لازم الوجود مما لا يستحيل انفكاكه من حيث هى هى ولا يلزم منه أن لا يستحيل انفكاكه منها من حيث الوجود أيضاً. يمتنع والحاصل: انا نعنى باللازم الذي هو مورد القسمة ما لا يستحيل انفكاكه عن الماهية في الجملة لا من حيث هي هي والايراد انما يأتي على ذلك التقدير دون الاول فان امتناع الانفكاك في الجملة يصدق بامتناعه من حيث هى هى وبامتناعه من حيث الوجود. ولو سلم فنقول : المراد بالماهية الماهية الموجودة فما يمتنع انفكاكه عنها اما ان يكون ممتنع الانفكاك من حيث هي هي اولا، الاول لازم الماهية و الثاني لازم الوجود. ثم التحقيق ان الايراد المذكور لا يرد على المحشى على الظاهر ايضاً و ان يدل لفظ الشيء و المعروض بلفظ الماهية حيث لاحظ في التقسيم قيد الحيثية المنبيء عن كون مورد القسمة هو لازم الماهية في الجملة المشتمل على القسمين المذكورين فلاحظ (محمد علی)
One of the verifying commentators among the glossators said: 'He only said "about its subject" and did not say "about the essence" as some others said, lest it be objected apparently that the division he mentions for the necessary concomitant is a division of a thing into itself (which is a necessary concomitant of the essence) and into other (which is a necessary concomitant of existence), for that which is not possible to separate from the essence? And with this in view, he took the subject of the division later as the necessary concomitant of the thing, not the necessary concomitant of the essence.' This is his statement, may his station be elevated. I say: 'He only said "apparently" because the mentioned objection does not rebut it in reality on the assumption that he says "about the essence" and takes the subject of division as necessary concomitant of the essence as well. For we do not grant that the necessary concomitant of existence is impossible to separate from it (the essence) in terms of it being what it is, and it does not follow that it is impossible to separate from it in terms of existence as well. It is impossible. The sum: we mean by the necessary concomitant which is the subject of division what is impossible to separate from the essence in general, not in terms of it being what it is. The objection only comes on that assumption, not the first. For the impossibility of separation in general is verified by its impossibility from the perspective of it being what it is and by its impossibility from the perspective of existence. And if it is granted, we say: the intent is the existing essence. So what is impossible to separate from it is either impossible to separate from it in terms of it being what it is or not. The first is necessary concomitant of the essence, the second is necessary concomitant of existence. Then the verification is that the mentioned objection does not rebut the glossator even apparently, even if the word "thing" and "subject" indicate the word "essence", since he considered in the division the qualification of perspective which indicates that the subject of division is the necessary concomitant of the essence in general, including the two mentioned divisions. So reflect. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection to a division of necessary concomitants. The objector argues that if the division is based on the necessary concomitant of the thing, it would divide a thing into itself (which is lazimum of the essence) and into something else (lazimum of existence). The response is that the division is about the necessary concomitant in a general sense, not restricted to the essence. The defender argues that the impossibility of separation from the essence in general covers both cases: impossible separation from the essence as such (lazimum of the essence) and impossible separation from the existence (lazimum of existence). This nuanced discussion shows the precision of terminology in Islamic logic.

Using 'subject' instead of 'essence' when dividing necessary concomitants prevents the logical error of dividing a thing into itself and another, preserving the validity of the logical classification.

Classifying Necessary Concomitants: Existence and Evidentness
Enumeration of divisions of necessary concomitants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify necessary concomitants according to the two divisions: by mode of existence and by evidentness.

Core Idea
Necessary concomitants are classified by existence and by evidentness.

A necessary concomitant (lazim) is inseparable from its subject. But not all necessary concomitants are the same. This passage introduces two ways to classify them: based on the mode of existence of the subject (essence, external, mental) and based on whether the relation is self-evident or requires proof. Understanding these divisions helps you analyse definitions and arguments that rely on concomitant properties.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish lazim from mufariq
Read the opening: 'what is impossible to separate from its subject is the necessary concomitant, and what is not impossible is the separable.' This sets the basic definition. A necessary concomitant is inseparable; a mufariq can separate.
2
Identify the first division: by subject's existence
The passage says the accident considered with existence of its subject divides into existence in the external world and existence in the mind. This gives three types: lazim al-mahiyya (essence), lazim al-wujud al-khariji (external existence), and lazim al-wujud al-dhihni (mental existence).
Lazim al-mahiyya is inseparable from the essence itself; the others depend on actual existence.
3
Identify the second division: by evidentness
The second division is bayyin (evident) vs ghayr bayyin (non-evident). The passage notes this resolves into further subdivisions: bayyin bi-l-ma'na al-akhass (specific sense) and bi-l-ma'na al-a'amm (general sense).
Bayyin means the concomitance is immediately known; ghayr bayyin requires proof.
4
Recognize cross-cutting classifications
The passage notes that all divisions of necessary concomitants must enter into each of these divisions. That is, any lazim can be classified by both existence and evidentness simultaneously.
Example: the impossibility of contradiction is lazim al-mahiyya and bayyin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله فالاول هو الاول يَعنى ما يستحيل انفكاكه عن معروضه هو اللازم و ما لا يستحيل هو المفارق، قوله (وهذا القسم اى العرض المنظور به وجود معروضه قسمان باعتبار وجود المعروض فى الخارج ووجوده فى الذهن فاقسام اللازم فى هذا القسم من التقسيمين اللذين ذكرهما الشارح لللازم ثلاثة لازم الماهية ولازم الوجود الخارجى و لازم الوجود الذهني (التقريب ص (۳۶) بالفتح :اى بتقسيمين يقال: قسمت المال اقسم كاضرب قسماً بالفتح ، ای قسمته بين هذا اللازم و على والحاصل ان اللازم تقسيمين: التقسيم الأول انه اما لازم الماهية او لازم الوجود والثانى انه اما او غير بين و هذا ينحل فى الحقيقة بتقسيمين كما سيصرح به المحشى الاول انه اما بين بالمعنى الاخص او غير بين بالمعنى الاخص والثانى انه أما بين بالمعنى الاعم او غير بين بالمعنى الاعم. ولا يخفى انه يجب ان يدخل جميع اقسام اللوازم فى اقسام كل واحد من هذه التقسيمات فتأمل. (محمد على)
His statement 'the first is the first' means: what is impossible to separate from its subject is the necessary concomitant, and what is not impossible is the separable. His statement 'and this division, i.e., the accident considered with the existence of its subject is two types: with respect to the existence of the subject in the external world and its existence in the mind, so the divisions of the necessary concomitant in this division from the two divisions that the commentator mentioned for the necessary concomitant are three: necessary concomitant of the essence, necessary concomitant of external existence, and necessary concomitant of mental existence.' (Taqrib p. 36) 'With the fatha: i.e., with two divisions. One says: I divided the wealth, dividing like 'daraba' with fatha, i.e., I divided it. And the sum: the necessary concomitant has two divisions: The first division is that it is either a necessary concomitant of the essence or a necessary concomitant of existence. The second is that it is either evident (bayyin) or non-evident (ghayr bayyin). And this in reality resolves into two divisions as the glossator will explain: the first is that it is either evident in the specific sense or non-evident in the specific sense, and the second is that it is either evident in the general sense or non-evident in the general sense. And it is not hidden that all divisions of necessary concomitants must enter into the divisions of each one of these divisions. So reflect. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage provides a clear classification of necessary concomitants. First, it defines lazim as that which cannot be separated from its subject, and mufariq as that which can. Then it gives two divisions: first based on the mode of existence of the subject, resulting in three types: lazimum of essence, lazimum of external existence, and lazimum of mental existence. Second, based on evidentness, resulting in evident (bayyin) and non-evident (ghayr bayyin), with sub-divisions: evident in the specific sense, etc. This taxonomy is used in later discussions of definitions and proofs.

Distinguishing Mental Concomitance from Implicational Signification
Clarification that 'mental concomitance' in the classification does not refer to implicational signification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mental concomitance (luzum dhahni) and implicational signification (dalala iltizamiyya) in the classification of necessary concomitants, and explain why the distinction is necessary for an exhaustive division.

Core Idea
Mental concomitance is about co-occurrence in the mind, not conceptual necessity.

In studying the division of necessary concomitants, one encounters the term 'mental concomitance.' At first glance, it might seem to refer to the logical relation where one concept cannot be conceived without another—implicational signification. However, this passage from a hashia clarifies that mental concomitance has a narrower meaning: it simply means that whenever the subject occurs in the mind, the concomitant also occurs, without implying conceptual inseparability. Understanding this nuance prevents the division from collapsing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two relations
First, grasp the two relations at play: mental concomitance (that the subject cannot occur in the mind without the concomitant) and implicational signification (that one cannot conceive the subject without the concomitant). The passage explicitly distinguishes them.
Mental concomitance is about occurrence; implicational signification is about conception.
2
See the consequence of conflation
If mental concomitance were equated with implicational signification, the division would not be distinct because external concomitants and essential concomitants could also be mentally concomitant in that sense. Similarly, the division would not be exhaustive because mental concomitants like universality would fall outside the categories.
The commentator uses two arguments: loss of distinctness and loss of exhaustiveness.
3
Recognize the correct definition
The correct meaning is 'the impossibility of the occurrence of that thing in the mind separated from the occurrence of this concomitant in it'—the concomitant occurs in the mind by itself (bi-nafsihi), not merely by its form (bi-suratihi). This excludes conceptual necessity.
The phrase 'by itself, not by its form' is key—it means the actual concomitant, not its mental image.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله كان هذا اللازم ثابتاً له : اى يمتنع ذلك الشيء منفكا عن هذا فمعنى كونه لازماً للشيء بالنظر الى وجوده الخارجي، هو ان يكون ذلك الشيء بحيث كلما تحقق في الذهن كان هذا اللازم ثابتاً له على معنى انه يمتنع حصول ذلك الشيء منفكاً عن هذا اللازم وليس المراد باللزوم الذهني هنا اللزوم المعتبر في الدلالة الالتزامية بمعنى ان يمتنع ادراك ذلك الشيء بدون ادراك هذا اللازم والا لم يكن الاقسام متباينة، لان اللازم باللزوم الخارجى ولازم الماهية ايضاً يجوز ان يكون لازماً ذهناً بالمعنى المذكور و ايضاً لو كان المراد باللزوم المعنى المذكور لم تكن القسمة حاصرة لان لزوم اللوازم الذهنية التى وجوداتها الاصلية تابعة لوجود ملزوماتها في الذهن فقط كالكلية والجنسية والنوعية و الفصلية وغيرها خارج عن القسمين الأولين لان وجود ملزوماتها في الخارج ينفك عن وجوداتها فيه فلابد ان يكون داخلاً في القسم الثالث. فلو كان المراد من اللزوم المعتبر فيه المعنى المذكور، لم يكن داخلا فيه لان ادراك الملزوم ينفك عن ادراك اللازم فليس المراد من اللزوم فيه الا اللزوم بمعنى امتناع حصول ذلك الشيء في الذهن منفكاً عن حصول هذا اللازم فيه بنفسه لا بصورته . (عبد الرحيم)
His statement 'this necessary concomitant is established for it' means: it is impossible for that thing to be separated from this. So the meaning of its being a necessary concomitant for the thing with respect to its external existence is that that thing is such that whenever it is realized in the mind, this concomitant is established for it, in the sense that it is impossible for that thing to occur separated from this concomitant. And what is intended by mental concomitance here is not the concomitance considered in implicational signification (dalala iltizamiyya), meaning that it is impossible to perceive that thing without perceiving this concomitant; otherwise the divisions would not be distinct. For the necessary concomitant by external concomitance and the necessary concomitant of the essence may also be mentally concomitant in the mentioned sense. Also, if the intended meaning of the concomitance were that meaning, the division would not be exhaustive, because the concomitance of mental concomitants whose original existences are dependent only on the existence of their subjects in the mind, such as universality, genericness, specificness, differentiae, etc., are outside the first two divisions, because the existence of their subjects in the external world is separate from their existence in it. So they must be included in the third division. If the intended meaning of the considered concomitance were that meaning, they would not be included in it, because perception of the subject is separate from perception of the concomitant. So what is intended by the concomitance there is only the concomitance in the sense of the impossibility of the occurrence of that thing in the mind separated from the occurrence of this concomitant in it, by itself, not by its form. (Abd al-Rahim)
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This note clarifies an important point about the term 'mental concomitance' (luzum dhahni) in the classification of necessary concomitants. It distinguishes it from implicational signification (dalala iltizamiyya), which is a logical relation where one concept cannot be conceived without another. In the context of the division, mental concomitance refers to the fact that whenever the subject occurs in the mind, the concomitant also occurs, but this is not a logical necessity of conception. This distinction ensures that the division remains exhaustive: mental concomitants like universality are included in the third division (lazimum of mental existence) and are not confused with the other two types.

Key Terms
اللزوم الذهني
الدلالة الالتزامية

Key Takeaway
Mental concomitance means co-occurrence in the mind, not conceptual necessity; this distinction keeps the classification of necessary concomitants both distinct and exhaustive.

The Three Types of Necessary Concomitant
Author divides al-lazim into three types and clarifies mental concomitance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given necessary property of a thing as lazim al-mahiyya, lazim al-wujud al-khariji, or lazim al-wujud al-dhihni based on the mode of existence.

Core Idea
A thing's necessary concomitants differ based on essence, external existence, or mental existence.

In logic and Islamic philosophy, every thing has properties that necessarily accompany it, but not all are the same kind. Some belong to the essence, some only appear when the thing exists externally, and some only when it is conceived in the mind. Our author distinguishes these three types to avoid confusion in definitions and arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the thing and its properties
Take a concrete example from Islamic sciences, e.g., 'fire' and its property 'heat'. List the property and the thing.
2
Check if the property is inseparable from the thing's essence
If the property is part of what the thing is (its quiddity), it is lazim al-mahiyya. Example: 'rationality' for a human being is part of the essence.
Properties like genus and differentia are lazim al-mahiyya.
3
Check if the property depends on external existence
If the property is only present when the thing exists in external reality, it is lazim al-wujud al-khariji. Example: 'heat' accompanies fire only in external existence.
In the mind, fire can be conceived without heat.
4
Check if the property is only present in mental existence
If the property attaches only when the thing is conceived in the mind, it is lazim al-wujud al-dhihni. Example: 'universality' of the concept 'man' exists only in the mind.
External individual men are not universal.
5
Apply the classification to new examples
Practice with examples from usul al-fiqh or kalam, like 'being obligatory' for an act. Is it essence or existence?
Consider the mode of existence of the subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (فالاول هو الاول) یعنی ما يستحيل انفكاكه عن معروضه هو اللازم و ما لا يستحيل هو المفارق. قوله (وهذا القسم اى العرض المنظور به وجود معروضه قسمان باعتبار وجود المعروض فى الخارج ووجوده فى الذهن فاقسام اللازم فى هذا القسم من التقسيمين اللذين ذكرهما الشارح لللازم ثلاثة لازم الماهية ولازم الوجود الخارجى و لازم الوجود الذهني. بالفتح :اى بتقسيمين يقال: قسمت المال اقسم كاضرب قسماً بالفتح ، ای قسمته بين هذا اللازم و على. والحاصل ان اللازم تقسيمين: التقسيم الأول انه اما لازم الماهية او لازم الوجود والثانى انه اما او غير بين و هذا ينحل فى الحقيقة بتقسيمين كما سيصرح به المحشى الاول انه اما بين بالمعنى الاخص او غير بين بالمعنى الاخص والثانى انه أما بين بالمعنى الاعم او غير بين بالمعنى الاعم. ولا يخفى انه يجب ان يدخل جميع اقسام اللوازم فى اقسام كل واحد من هذه التقسيمات فتأمل. (محمد على) قوله كان هذا اللازم ثابتاً له : اى يمتنع ذلك الشيء منفكا عن هذا فمعنى كونه لازماً للشيء بالنظر الى وجوده الخارجي، هو ان يكون ذلك الشيء بحيث كلما تحقق في الذهن كان هذا اللازم ثابتاً له على معنى انه يمتنع حصول ذلك الشيء منفكاً عن هذا اللازم وليس المراد باللزوم الذهني هنا اللزوم المعتبر في الدلالة الالتزامية بمعنى ان يمتنع ادراك ذلك الشيء بدون ادراك هذا اللازم والا لم يكن الاقسام متباينة، لان اللازم باللزوم الخارجى ولازم الماهية ايضاً يجوز ان يكون لازماً ذهناً بالمعنى المذكور و ايضاً لو كان المراد باللزوم المعنى المذكور لم تكن القسمة حاصرة لان لزوم اللوازم الذهنية التى وجوداتها الاصلية تابعة لوجود ملزوماتها في الذهن فقط كالكلية والجنسية والنوعية و الفصلية وغيرها خارج عن القسمين الأولين لان وجود ملزوماتها في الخارج ينفك عن وجوداتها فيه فلابد ان يكون داخلاً في القسم الثالث. فلو كان المراد من اللزوم المعتبر فيه المعنى المذكور، لم يكن داخلا فيه لان ادراك الملزوم ينفك عن ادراك اللازم فليس المراد من اللزوم فيه الا اللزوم بمعنى امتناع حصول ذلك الشيء في الذهن منفكاً عن حصول هذا اللازم فيه بنفسه لا بصورته . (عبد الرحيم)
His saying: (The first is the first) means that what is impossible to separate from its subject is the necessary concomitant, and what is not impossible is the separable. His saying: (And this section, i.e., the accident considered with the existence of its substrate, is two divisions: in consideration of the existence of the substrate in external reality and its existence in the mind. So the divisions of the necessary concomitant in this section from the two divisions that the commentator mentioned for the necessary concomitant are three: the necessary concomitant of the essence, the necessary concomitant of external existence, and the necessary concomitant of mental existence.) With fatha: i.e., with two divisions. It is said: I divided the property, I divide it like 'daraba' as a division with fatha, i.e., its division between this necessary concomitant and [other?]. The summary: the necessary concomitant has two divisions: the first division is that it is either a necessary concomitant of the essence or a necessary concomitant of existence, and the second is that it is either evident or non-evident. This in reality resolves into two divisions, as the commentator will clarify: first, it is either evident in the specific sense or non-evident in the specific sense, and second, it is either evident in the general sense or non-evident in the general sense. And it is not hidden that all divisions of necessary concomitants must enter into the divisions of each one of these divisions, so consider. (Muhammad ‘Ali) His saying: (This necessary concomitant is established for it) means that it is impossible for that thing to be separated from this. So the meaning of its being a necessary concomitant for the thing with respect to its external existence is that that thing is such that whenever it is actualized in the mind, this necessary concomitant is established for it, meaning that it is impossible for that thing to occur separated from this necessary concomitant. And what is intended by mental concomitance here is not the concomitance considered in implicative signification, meaning that it is impossible to perceive that thing without perceiving this necessary concomitant, because then the divisions would not be mutually exclusive, for the necessary concomitant by external concomitance and the necessary concomitant of the essence could also be mental concomitants in that sense. Also, if what was intended by concomitance was that meaning, the division would not be exhaustive, because the concomitance of mental necessary concomitants whose original existences follow the existence of their subjects only in the mind, such as universality, genusness, speciesness, differentianess, and others, falls outside the first two divisions, because their subjects' existence in external reality is separate from their existences there. So it must be included in the third division. Thus, if what was intended by the considered concomitance was that meaning, it would not be included in it, because the perception of the subject is separate from the perception of the necessary concomitant. So what is intended by concomitance in it is only concomitance in the sense of the impossibility of that thing occurring in the mind separated from this concomitant occurring in it by itself, not by its form. (‘Abd al-Rahim)
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the necessary concomitant (lazim). The author begins by distinguishing the necessary from the separable: if separation is impossible, it is necessarry; if not, it is separable. Then, focusing on the accident considered insofar as its substrate exists, he mentions that this accident is divided according to whether the substrate exists externally or mentally. Accordingly, the necessarry concomitants in this context are three: (1) necessary concomitant of the essence (lazim al-mahiyya), which attaches to the thing's quiddity regardless of existence; (2) necessary concomitant of external existence (lazim al-wujud al-khariji), which attaches when the thing exists in the external world; and (3) necessary concomitant of mental existence (lazim al-wujud al-dhihni), which attaches only when the thing is conceived in the mind. The text then notes that the necessarry concomitant is further divided into two schemes: one based on whether it attaches to essence or existence, and another based on whether it is evident (bayyin) or non-evident (ghayr bayyin), with each scheme having two subdivisions (specific and general senses of 'evident'). The author emphasizes that all types of necessarry concomitants must be covered by these divisions. Finally, he clarifies the meaning of mental concomitance: it is the impossibility of the thing occurring in the mind without its concomitant, but not in the sense of implicative signification (dalalat iltizamiyya), because that would make the divisions overlap and fail to include mental concomitants like universality. The example: for a universal like 'man', its universality is a mental concomitant; it is impossible to have 'man' in the mind without it being a universal, but this is not a matter of implication.

Key Terms
اللازم
لازم الماهية
لازم الوجود الخارجي
لازم الوجود الذهني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of lazim al-wujud al-khariji?
A) The rationality of a human being
B) The heat of fire when fire exists externally
C) The universality of the concept 'man'
D) The possibility (imkan) of the world

Key Takeaway
The three types of necessary concomitant are distinguished by whether the property attaches to the essence (mahiyya), to external existence (khariji), or to mental existence (dhihni).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
For the concept 'iman (faith)' in the mind, identify which type of lazim the property 'being a concept' belongs to and explain why.
?
In a usul al-fiqh discussion, the property 'being abrogating (nasikh)' is a necessary concomitant of a ruling. Determine whether this is lazim al-mahiyya, lazim al-wujud al-khariji, or lazim al-wujud al-dhihni, and justify your answer.

Mental Concomitance vs. Implicative Signification
Author clarifies the meaning of mental concomitance and excludes implicative signification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish mental concomitance (al-luzum al-dhihni) from implicative signification (dalalat iltizamiyya) and explain why the two must not be conflated.

Core Idea
Mental concomitance is about the impossibility of separation in the mind; implicative signification is about understanding one concept from another.

The third type of necessary concomitant (lazim al-wujud al-dhihni) involves a property that attaches to a thing only when it is conceived in the mind. But what does 'necessary' mean here? The author clarifies that it is not the same as implicative signification, where one meaning implies another. Rather, it is the impossibility of the thing being present in the mind without its concomitant also being present, even if we do not explicitly think of the concomitant.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of mental concomitance
Mental concomitance is the impossibility of a thing occurring in the mind without its concomitant. Example: the concept 'man' cannot be conceived without having the property 'being a universal'.
This is not about understanding 'man' leading to understanding 'universal' explicitly.
2
Contrast with implicative signification
Implicative signification is when understanding one concept leads to understanding another, e.g., understanding 'father' implies understanding 'child'. But the father can be in the mind without the child being explicitly understood.
In mental concomitance, the concomitant is present but not necessarily perceived.
3
Why this distinction matters for the classification
If mental concomitance were the same as implicative signification, then the three types would overlap (external concomitants also imply something) and the division would not be exhaustive (mental concomitants like universality would be excluded).
The commentator explicitly argues this point.
4
Apply to an example from Islamic logic
Consider the concept 'existence'. Its mental concomitant is 'being a concept'. But we do not need to think of that to understand existence. This is a mental concomitant, not a case of implicative signification.
Distinguish from the implication that existence implies a subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Two Senses of Evident Concomitance
Defines two senses of evident concomitance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the specific and general senses of evident concomitance and identify the criterion for each.

Core Idea
Evident concomitance comes in two degrees: specific (subject alone suffices) and general (both terms and relation needed).

This passage from al-Yazdi's Hashiya on al-Taqrib clarifies the levels of evident (bayyin) necessary concomitance. Understanding these degrees is crucial for analysing implicative signification (dalalat al-iltizam) in Islamic logic and its applications in kalam and usul al-fiqh. We will dissect the definitions and see how they apply to examples like blindness and sight.

Guided Path
1
Identify the specific sense criterion
Find where the author defines al-bayyin bi’l-ma‘na al-akhass: it requires that the conception of the subject (al-malzum) alone suffices both to conceive the concomitant (al-lazim) and to be certain of the concomitance.
No additional terms or relations needed.
2
Identify the general sense criterion
For al-bayyin bi’l-ma‘na al-a‘amm, the conception of the subject alone is not enough for certainty; you must also conceive the concomitant and the relation between them.
Certainty, not mere conception, is the key.
3
Apply to an example
Consider blindness and sight: from the concept of blindness, you immediately know it entails lack of sight (specific evident). But is the converse also specific evident? No, because sight does not entail blindness. In general sense, to be certain of any mental concomitance, you may need both concepts.
This shows the distinction in practice.
4
Differentiate the two senses
The specific sense is stronger: anything specific is automatically general, but not vice versa. The author also notes that the general sense is not necessarily broader in terms of certainty—a crucial nuance.
Generality here is not absolute; it depends on the conceptual scope.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله و الثاني: عطف على قوله : «ثم اللازم ينقسم بقسمين احدهما». (التقريب ص ۳۷) هذا هو اللزوم الذهنى المعتبر فى الدلالة الالتزامية (عبد الرحيم) اى وحين اذ عرفت البين بالمعنى الاخص فغير البين منه هو اللازم الذي لا يلزم تصوره من تصور الملزوم. (التقريب ص ۳۷) لانه كلما يكفى تصور الملزوم فى اللزوم يكفى تصور اللازم مع تصور الملزوم و النسبة بينهما قانه اذا كان تصور العمى مثلاً كافياً فى لزوم البصر له فلاريب انه يكفى : هما مع تصور النسبة بينهما في تصور الممزوم. تصور اللازم وفيه انه قد علم سابقاً ان المعتبر فى البين بالمعنى الاخص هو بالمعنى الاخص هو كون تصور الملزوم كافياً فى تصور الـ مع الجزم باللزوم فيمكن ان يكون تصور الملزوم كافياً فى تصور اللازم ولا يكون تصور هما مع تصور النسبة كافياً في الجزم باللزوم بل يحتاج فيه الى واسطة فلا يكون البين بالمعنى الثاني اعم من البين بالمعنى الأول. الا ان يحمل العموم والخصوص على ما هو بحسب المفهوم (عبد الرحيم) (۱۳۸) قوله و حينئذ اى وحين اذ عرفت البين بالمعنى الاعم فغير البين منه هو اللازم الذى لا يلزم من تصوره مع تصور الملزوم و النسبة بينهما الجزم باللزوم. (التقريب ص ۳۷)
His saying: (And the second) is coordinated to his saying: 'Then the necessary concomitant divides into two divisions, one of them...' (al-Taqrib p. 37) This is the mental concomitance considered in implicative signification. (‘Abd al-Rahim) i.e., when you have known the evident in the specific sense, then the non-evident from it is the necessary concomitant whose conception does not follow from the conception of the subject. (al-Taqrib p. 37) Because whenever the conception of the subject suffices for the concomitance, the conception of the concomitant with the conception of the subject and the relation between them also suffices. For if the conception of blindness, for example, suffices for the necessary concomitance of sight to it, then there is no doubt that both of them together with the conception of the relation between them suffice for the conception of the subject? [sic] And in it, it has been previously known that what is considered in the evident in the specific sense is that the conception of the subject suffices for the conception of the concomitant together with certainty of concomitance. So it is possible that the conception of the subject suffices for the conception of the concomitant, but the conception of both together with the conception of the relation does not suffice for certainty of concomitance; rather it needs an intermediary. Thus the evident in the second sense is not more general than the evident in the first sense, unless we interpret generality and particularity according to what is by concept. (‘Abd al-Rahim) (138) His saying: (And at that point) i.e., when you have known the evident in the general sense, then the non-evident from it is the necessary concomitant whose conception together with the conception of the subject and the relation between them does not necessitate certainty of concomitance. (al-Taqrib p. 37)
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage defines the evident (bayyin) and non-evident (ghayr bayyin) for necessary concomitance, distinguishing two senses. First, the evident in the specific sense (bayyin bi’l-ma‘na al-akhass): the conception of the subject alone is sufficient to conceive the concomitant and to be certain of the concomitance. For example, from the concept of blindness, you immediately conceive sight as its concomitant and are certain that blindness implies sight but not vice versa? Actually the example given is that if blindness suffices to establish sight, then that is evident in the specific sense. Second, the evident in the general sense (bayyin bi’l-ma‘na al-a‘amm): here, the conception of the subject alone is not sufficient; you need to conceive both the subject and the concomitant together with the relation between them to be certain of the concomitance. The author then notes that the evident in the general sense is not necessarily more general than the specific sense when considering certainty, because even if the subject alone suffices for conceiving the concomitant, it might not suffice for certainty without an intermediary. The key point: whatever is evident in the specific sense is also evident in the general sense, but not vice versa. The non-evident (ghayr bayyin) is that which does not yield certainty even after conceiving both terms and the relation; it requires an additional medium.

Key Terms
البين بالمعنى الأخص
البين بالمعنى الأعم
غير البين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes al-bayyin bi’l-ma‘na al-akhass from al-bayyin bi’l-ma‘na al-a‘amm?
A) Only the specific sense requires certainty of concomitance.
B) The specific sense requires only subject-conception for both conception and certainty, while the general sense requires both terms and relation for certainty.
C) The general sense does not require any conception of the subject.
D) The specific sense is not applicable to mental concomitance.

Key Takeaway
Evident concomitance has two levels: specific (subject alone gives conception and certainty) and general (subject, concomitant, and relation needed for certainty); non-evident always needs an intermediary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following relation as bayyin bi’l-ma‘na al-akhass, bayyin bi’l-ma‘na al-a‘amm, or ghayr bayyin: 'The world is temporally originated (huduth) implies it has a creator.' Consider whether conceiving 'temporal origination' alone gives certainty of a creator.
?
Find a real example from usul al-fiqh where a dalalat al-iltizam (implicative signification) is evident in the general sense but not specific. Explain why.

The Non-Evident Concomitant and Generality Nuance
Defines non-evident and refines the generality of evident senses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a non-evident concomitant and explain why the general sense of evident is not necessarily more general than the specific sense.

Core Idea
Non-evident concomitance requires an intermediary; the general sense is not broader when considering certainty.

Building on the two senses of evident, this lesson unpacks the non-evident (ghayr bayyin) and a key nuance: although the general sense of evident is usually thought to encompass the specific, the author argues that in terms of the certainty condition, it may not be more general. We will trace his reasoning and see its implications for logical inference.

Guided Path
1
Define the non-evident (ghayr bayyin)
Locate the definition: non-evident is a necessary concomitant whose certainty does not follow even after conceiving both the subject and the concomitant and the relation; it requires an additional intermediary (wāsiṭa), like a syllogism.
2
Understand the author's argument on generality
The author states: it is possible that the subject alone suffices for conceiving the concomitant (so concomitant is specific-evident in conception), but even adding the concomitant and relation does not give certainty; thus the general sense is not broader in terms of certainty.
This shows that 'evident in general sense' does not automatically include all cases of 'evident in specific sense' when considering certainty.
3
Apply the nuance
Consider the example of blindness and sight: from blindness you conceive sight (specific evident in conception?), but does the pair (blindness, sight) plus relation give certainty that blindness entails sight? Yes, but the author is considering a case where subject-conception gives conception but not certainty. Example: 'human' conceives 'rationality' immediately, but to be certain of the concomitance you might need to consider essence? Actually the author leaves the example open.
The nuance is subtle: it depends on whether 'certainty' is about the existence of the concomitance or the necessity.
4
Interpret the qualification 'by concept'
The author says unless we interpret generality and particularity 'according to what is by concept' (bi hasab al-mafhūm). This means if we consider the definitions themselves, the general sense concept is broader (includes more cases) than the specific sense concept, but in actual certainty they may coincide.
This resolves the seeming contradiction.

Source Text

(التقريب ص ۳۷)
His saying: (And the second) is coordinated to his saying: 'Then the necessary concomitant divides into two divisions, one of them...' (al-Taqrib p. 37) This is the mental concomitance considered in implicative signification. (‘Abd al-Rahim) i.e., when you have known the evident in the specific sense, then the non-evident from it is the necessary concomitant whose conception does not follow from the conception of the subject. (al-Taqrib p. 37) Because whenever the conception of the subject suffices for the concomitance, the conception of the concomitant with the conception of the subject and the relation between them also suffices. For if the conception of blindness, for example, suffices for the necessary concomitance of sight to it, then there is no doubt that both of them together with the conception of the relation between them suffice for the conception of the subject? [sic] And in it, it has been previously known that what is considered in the evident in the specific sense is that the conception of the subject suffices for the conception of the concomitant together with certainty of concomitance. So it is possible that the conception of the subject suffices for the conception of the concomitant, but the conception of both together with the conception of the relation does not suffice for certainty of concomitance; rather it needs an intermediary. Thus the evident in the second sense is not more general than the evident in the first sense, unless we interpret generality and particularity according to what is by concept. (‘Abd al-Rahim) (138) His saying: (And at that point) i.e., when you have known the evident in the general sense, then the non-evident from it is the necessary concomitant whose conception together with the conception of the subject and the relation between them does not necessitate certainty of concomitance. (al-Taqrib p. 37)
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage defines the evident (bayyin) and non-evident (ghayr bayyin) for necessary concomitance, distinguishing two senses. First, the evident in the specific sense (bayyin bi’l-ma‘na al-akhass): the conception of the subject alone is sufficient to conceive the concomitant and to be certain of the concomitance. For example, from the concept of blindness, you immediately conceive sight as its concomitant and are certain that blindness implies sight but not vice versa? Actually the example given is that if blindness suffices to establish sight, then that is evident in the specific sense. Second, the evident in the general sense (bayyin bi’l-ma‘na al-a‘amm): here, the conception of the subject alone is not sufficient; you need to conceive both the subject and the concomitant together with the relation between them to be certain of the concomitance. The author then notes that the evident in the general sense is not necessarily more general than the specific sense when considering certainty, because even if the subject alone suffices for conceiving the concomitant, it might not suffice for certainty without an intermediary. The key point: whatever is evident in the specific sense is also evident in the general sense, but not vice versa. The non-evident (ghayr bayyin) is that which does not yield certainty even after conceiving both terms and the relation; it requires an additional medium.

واسطة
عموم
خصوص

B) Because it is possible that the subject alone suffices for conception of the concomitant but the addition of concomitant and relation does not suffice for certainty, so the general sense does not cover that case of certainty.
C) Because the general sense only applies to non-mental concomitance.
D) Because the specific sense is actually broader in scope.

Key Takeaway
Non-evident concomitance always requires an external proof; the general sense of evident is not broader than the specific in terms of the certainty condition, only by conceptual definition.
