The Four Relations Between Definer and Defined
Author enumerates four possible relations between definer and defined and deduces that only equality is valid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the four possible relations between definer and defined and explain why only equality is valid for definition.

Core Idea
A valid definition requires the definer to be equal to the defined in predication.

In this passage, the author systematically lists all possible ways a definition (definer) can relate to the thing defined. By eliminating three invalid relations, he shows that only equality remains. This is a foundational principle for constructing correct definitions.

Guided Path
1
List the four relations
Identify the four types the author mentions: equality, mutual exclusion, absolute universal-particular, and universal-particular from one aspect.
2
Rule out mutual exclusion
Understand that if the definer and defined are mutually exclusive, the definer cannot be predicated of the defined at all, so it fails as a definition.
3
Rule out absolute universal-particular
If the definer is absolute universal and defined is particular, the definer applies to more than the defined, violating the requirement of equivalence.
4
Rule out universal-particular from one aspect
If they intersect partially, some instances of defined may not be covered by definer, and vice versa, so equivalence fails.
5
Conclude equality
Since the other three are invalid, only equality remains. Equality means mutual universal predication: the defined applies to every individual of the definer and vice versa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعرف والمعرف بعدما لم يكن عينه لئلا يلزم ان يكون الشيء معلوماً قبل العلم به متصورة باربعة انواع : الاول ان يكونا متساوبين والثانى ان يكونا متباينين. والثالث: ان يكونا اعم واخص مطلقاً. والرابع: ان يكونا اعم واخص من وجه. وقد تقرر في ماسبق عدم جواز الاخيرين وعلم من تعريفه بـ ما يقال عليه عدم جواز الثاني ايضاً لان مباين الشيء لا يحمل عليه فقد تعين الأول وهذا معنى قوله فتعين ان يكون مساو يا نه وقد عرفت فيما ان المساواة راجعة الى موجبتين كليتين فلابد فى صحة التعريف من صدق قضيتين موجبتين كليتين: احديهما: صدق المحدود على جميع مصاديق الحد وحمله عليه كلياً. و ثانيتهما عكسه اعني صدق الحد على جميع مصاديق المحدود و حمله عليه كلياً ومن ذلك ما اصطلحوا عليه من أنهم يعبرون عن كون الحد ما نعاً عن الاغيار بالاطراد كما يعبرون بالمنع وعن كونه جامعاً لافراد المحدود بالانعكاس كما يعبرون بالجمع و ذلك لاطراد صدق المحدود على مصاديق الحد على الاول و بالعكس على الثاني. فالمراد بالاطراد ان لا يكون شيء مصاديق الحد الا و يصدق عليه المحدود و بالانعكاس ان لا يكون شيء من مصاديق المحدود الأو يصدق عليه الحد. و من هنا يتبين ان ليس مرادهم بالعكس العكس الاصطلاحي لما سيجيء من ان عكس الموجبة لا يكون الاجزئية. ثم انما اعتبروا الاول طرداً و الثانى عكساً دون العكس، ضرورة ان المعتبر في صحة التعريف حال الحد فى مساواته للمحدود فالاولى ان يجعل موضوعاً فى الكلية الأولى فيؤخذ فى الثانية عكسها هذا. وقد يقال ان الاطراد من الطرد بمعنى المنع لمنعه عن دخول الاغيار فيه فالانعكاس في مقابل ذلك هو كون الحد جامعاً لافراد المحدود فتأمل.
The definer and the defined, after it was not the same, in order to avoid the thing being known before knowledge of it, are of four types: first, that they are equal; second, that they are mutually exclusive; third, that they are absolute universal and particular; fourth, that they are universal and particular from one aspect. It has been established previously that the last two are not permissible, and it is known from its definition by what is said about it that the second is also not permissible because what is mutually exclusive of a thing is not predicated of it. Thus the first is determined, and this is the meaning of his statement: 'So it is determined that it be equal.' And you have known from what preceded that equality reduces to two universal affirmatives. Therefore, the validity of the definition requires the truth of two universal affirmative propositions: one of them: the truth of the defined about all individuals of the definer and its predication on all of them; and the second is its converse, meaning the truth of the definer about all individuals of the defined and its predication on all of them. And from that comes their convention that they express the definer's preventing other things by 'ittiraad', just as they express by 'al-man' (prevention), and its comprehensiveness for individuals of the defined by 'in'ikaas', as they express by 'al-jam' (inclusion). That is because of the universal applicability of the truth of the defined about individuals of the definer in the first, and the opposite in the second. By 'ittiraad' is meant that there is no individual of the definer except that the defined is true of it, and by 'in'ikaas' that there is no individual of the defined except that the definer is true of it. From this it is clear that by 'in'ikaas' they do not mean the technical conversion, because (as will come) the converse of an affirmative is only particular. Then they considered the first as 'tard' and the second as 'aks', not the converse, because what is considered in the validity of the definition is the state of the definer in its equality to the defined. So it is more appropriate to make it the subject in the first universal, and take its converse in the second. This is it. And it is said that 'ittiraad' comes from 'tard' meaning prevention, because it prevents the entry of other things into it, and 'in'ikaas' in contrast is the definer being comprehensive of individuals of the defined. So reflect.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage defines the required relationship between the definer (mu'arrif) and the defined (mu'arraf) in a valid definition. The author states that the definer and defined, after not being identical, must be related in one of four ways: (1) equal (musawi), (2) mutually exclusive (mutabaayin), (3) absolute universal-particular (a'amm wa akhass mutlaq), (4) universal-particular from one aspect (a'amm wa akhass min wajh). The latter two have already been ruled out because the definer must be predicable of the defined. The second is also ruled out because things that are mutually exclusive cannot be predicated of each other. Therefore, only equality remains. This equality is understood as the mutual universal affirmative predication: the defined applies to every instance of the definer, and the definer applies to every instance of the defined. This is expressed in the terminology of ittirad (universal applicability of the defined over the definer) and in'ikas (converse universal applicability of the definer over the defined). The author clarifies that their use of 'in'ikas' is not the technical conversion which yields only a particular proposition. The condition of ittirad prevents the definition from including anything outside the defined, and in'ikas ensures it covers all instances of the defined.

المعرف (al-mu'arraf)
المعرف (al-mu'arrif)
المساواة (al-musawa)
المتباين (al-mutabaayin)
الأعم والأخص مطلقاً
الأعم والأخص من وجه

Key Takeaway
A valid definition requires the definer and defined to be equal in extension, i.e., coextensive universal predication.

Conditions of Ittiraad and In'ikaas in Definition
Author explains the conditions of ittiraad and in'ikaas required for a valid definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the conditions of ittiraad (prevention of extraneous) and in'ikaas (comprehensiveness) to test the validity of a definition.

Core Idea
A valid definition must prevent extraneous entries and cover all instances.

Having established that equality is required, the author unpacks it into two conditions: ittiraad (the defined applies to every individual of the definer) and in'ikaas (the definer applies to every individual of the defined). These correspond to the traditional terms 'man'' (prevention) and 'jam''' (inclusion). Understanding these conditions helps you evaluate any definition.

Guided Path
1
Recall the equality requirement
Remember that a valid definition requires two universal affirmative propositions: defined on definer and definer on defined.
2
Define ittiraad
Ittiraad means that the defined is true of every individual of the definer. This prevents the definer from including anything outside the defined (extraneous).
It comes from 'tard' meaning prevention.
3
Define in'ikaas
In'ikaas means that the definer is true of every individual of the defined. This ensures the definer covers all instances of the defined (comprehensiveness).
This is not the technical conversion ('aks) which yields only a particular proposition.
4
Note the terminology
The author mentions that some scholars use 'man'' (prevention) for ittiraad and 'jam''' (inclusion) for in'ikaas. Both pairs mean the same conditions.
5
Test a definition
Apply these conditions to a candidate definition: check if ittiraad holds (the defined applies to all instances of the definer) and if in'ikaas holds (the definer applies to all instances of the defined).

المعرف والمعرف بعدما لم يكن عينه لئلا يلزم ان يكون الشيء معلوماً قبل العلم به متصورة باربعة انواع : الاول ان يكونا متساوبين والثانى ان يكونا متباينين. والثالث: ان يكونا اعم واخص مطلقاً. والرابع: ان يكونا اعم واخص من وجه. وقد تقرر في ماسبق عدم جواز الاخيرين وعلم من تعريفه بـ ما يقال عليه عدم جواز الثاني ايضاً لان مباين الشيء لا يحمل عليه فقد تعين الأول وهذا معنى قوله فتعين ان يكون مساو يا نه وقد عرفت فيما ان المساواة راجعة الى موجبتين كليتين فلابد فى صحة التعريف من صدق قضيتين موجبتين كليتين: احديهما: صدق المحدود على جميع مصاديق الحد وحمله عليه كلياً. و ثانيتهما عكسه اعني صدق الحد على جميع مصاديق المحدود و حمله عليه كلياً ومن ذلك ما اصطلحوا عليه من أنهم يعبرون عن كون الحد ما نعاً عن الاغيار بالاطراد كما يعبرون بالمنع وعن كونه جامعاً لافراد المحدود بالانعكاس كما يعبرون بالجمع و ذلك لاطراد صدق المحدود على مصاديق الحد على الاول و بالعكس على الثاني. فالمراد بالاطراد ان لا يكون شيء مصاديق الحد الا و يصدق عليه المحدود و بالانعكاس ان لا يكون شيء من مصاديق المحدود الأو يصدق عليه الحد. و من هنا يتبين ان ليس مرادهم بالعكس العكس الاصطلاحي لما سيجيء من ان عكس الموجبة لا يكون الاجزئية. ثم انما اعتبروا الاول طرداً و الثانى عكساً دون العكس، ضرورة ان المعتبر في صحة التعريف حال الحد فى مساواته للمحدود فالاولى ان يجعل موضوعاً فى الكلية الأولى فيؤخذ فى الثانية عكسها هذا. وقد يقال ان الاطراد من الطرد بمعنى المنع لمنعه عن دخول الاغيار فيه فالانعكاس في مقابل ذلك هو كون الحد جامعاً لافراد المحدود فتأمل.
The definer and the defined, after it was not the same, in order to avoid the thing being known before knowledge of it, are of four types: first, that they are equal; second, that they are mutually exclusive; third, that they are absolute universal and particular; fourth, that they are universal and particular from one aspect. It has been established previously that the last two are not permissible, and it is known from its definition by what is said about it that the second is also not permissible because what is mutually exclusive of a thing is not predicated of it. Thus the first is determined, and this is the meaning of his statement: 'So it is determined that it be equal.' And you have known from what preceded that equality reduces to two universal affirmatives. Therefore, the validity of the definition requires the truth of two universal affirmative propositions: one of them: the truth of the defined about all individuals of the definer and its predication on all of them; and the second is its converse, meaning the truth of the definer about all individuals of the defined and its predication on all of them. And from that comes their convention that they express the definer's preventing other things by 'ittiraad', just as they express by 'al-man' (prevention), and its comprehensiveness for individuals of the defined by 'in'ikaas', as they express by 'al-jam' (inclusion). That is because of the universal applicability of the truth of the defined about individuals of the definer in the first, and the opposite in the second. By 'ittiraad' is meant that there is no individual of the definer except that the defined is true of it, and by 'in'ikaas' that there is no individual of the defined except that the definer is true of it. From this it is clear that by 'in'ikaas' they do not mean the technical conversion, because (as will come) the converse of an affirmative is only particular. Then they considered the first as 'tard' and the second as 'aks', not the converse, because what is considered in the validity of the definition is the state of the definer in its equality to the defined. So it is more appropriate to make it the subject in the first universal, and take its converse in the second. This is it. And it is said that 'ittiraad' comes from 'tard' meaning prevention, because it prevents the entry of other things into it, and 'in'ikaas' in contrast is the definer being comprehensive of individuals of the defined. So reflect.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage defines the required relationship between the definer (mu'arrif) and the defined (mu'arraf) in a valid definition. The author states that the definer and defined, after not being identical, must be related in one of four ways: (1) equal (musawi), (2) mutually exclusive (mutabaayin), (3) absolute universal-particular (a'amm wa akhass mutlaq), (4) universal-particular from one aspect (a'amm wa akhass min wajh). The latter two have already been ruled out because the definer must be predicable of the defined. The second is also ruled out because things that are mutually exclusive cannot be predicated of each other. Therefore, only equality remains. This equality is understood as the mutual universal affirmative predication: the defined applies to every instance of the definer, and the definer applies to every instance of the defined. This is expressed in the terminology of ittirad (universal applicability of the defined over the definer) and in'ikas (converse universal applicability of the definer over the defined). The author clarifies that their use of 'in'ikas' is not the technical conversion which yields only a particular proposition. The condition of ittirad prevents the definition from including anything outside the defined, and in'ikas ensures it covers all instances of the defined.

Key Terms
الاطراد (al-ittiraad)
الانعكاس (al-in'ikaas)
المنع (al-man')
الجمع (al-jam')
المساواة (al-musawa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does 'in'ikaas' refer to in the context of definition as explained in the text?
A) The logical conversion of an affirmative proposition to a particular
B) The condition that the definer is predicated of every individual of the defined
C) The condition that the defined is predicated of every individual of the definer
D) The mutual exclusivity of definer and defined

Key Takeaway
A valid definition must satisfy both ittiraad (prevention of extraneous) and in'ikaas (comprehensiveness), ensuring coextensive universal predication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Test the definition 'Zakat is 2.5% of surplus wealth held for one lunar year.' Does it satisfy ittiraad and in'ikaas? Identify which condition might be violated and provide a counterexample.
?
In Kalam, a theologian defines 'qadim' (eternal) as 'that which has no beginning.' Apply the conditions of ittiraad and in'ikaas to check if this definition is valid. (Hint: Consider whether everything eternal truly has no beginning and whether everything without beginning is eternal.)

The Definer Must Be More Known Than the Defined
Condition that definer be more known than defined

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition that the definer must be more known than the defined and explain why simultaneous or posterior knowledge of the definer invalidates a definition.

Core Idea
The definer must be known before the defined, or definition fails.

In this lesson, we examine a fundamental condition of definition: the definer (al-mu'arraf) must be more known (a'raf) than the defined (al-mu'arraf). The passage explains why this is necessary—otherwise either both are known simultaneously or the definer is known after the defined, both of which prevent the definer from serving as a valid definition—and resolves an apparent contradiction with the earlier condition of equality by distinguishing two meanings of 'equality'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
Locate the phrase 'ثم ينبغى ان يكون المعرّف اعرف من المعرّف'. This is the condition under discussion.
2
Understand the justification
The author gives two problematic scenarios: (1) both are known simultaneously—then making one a definer is arbitrary; (2) the definer is known after the defined—then it cannot be a definer because it is unknown at the time.
3
Reconcile with the equality condition
The author explains that 'equality' here means equality in obscurity/clarity, not in universality/particularity. This avoids contradiction with earlier statements about equality.

His statement: 'Then the definer should be more known than the defined.' I say: It should also be known before the definer, otherwise either knowledge of both would be simultaneous or knowledge of the definer would be posterior to knowledge of the defined; and in either case the definer would be a definer of the defined. As for the first, since knowledge of both is simultaneous, making one a definer of the other is not more appropriate than the converse. As for the second, because what is not known cannot be a definer of the known. (Abd al-Rahim) Then he qualified it with that, to avoid misunderstanding of contradiction between this and his statement 'so it is determined that it be equal', because the intended meaning of equality here is equality in obscurity and clarity, while there equality is in universality and particularity; and an indication that equality in knowledge necessitates equality in ignorance, so there is no need to qualify equality with both matters. Thus no objection arises against the author that he should say 'and the equal in knowledge and ignorance' as is the expression of the later scholars. Then he delayed the equal after the more obscure, because the shorter is more deserving of precedence, and the text reversed it for the sake of harmony. (Muhammad Ali) (12) You have known the discussion on that condition, so we say: The definition including a matter specific to the defined is not necessary; rather definition by a common accident is possible if the purpose is to distinguish the thing from some of what is other than it, though consideration of it in the chapter of definition is not commendable. However, what they mentioned of the condition of equality only requires that the common accident not be a definition, not that it not be a part of the definition before the common accident, inasmuch as being a common accident does not provide distinction. For example, something as a common accident does not provide distinction at all, but rather as an accidental special property. (Abd al-Rahim) (13) Because the essentials as mentioned are three: genus, species, and differentia. It has been known previously that the species cannot be a definition because it is more specific, likewise the genus absolutely whether proximate or distant because it is more general, and similarly the distant differentia. Thus the proximate differentia is determined. (Muhammad Ali) (14) Because the accident as preceded is either a common accident or a special property, and it will come that they do not consider the common accident, so the special property is determined. Then it is not hidden that it is permissible that his statement 'necessarily' be a qualifier for each of his statements 'it was a proximate differentia' and 'it was a special property', so reflect. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (15) His statement: 'So according to the first, the definer is called hadd because hadd in language means prevention, and because of its specificity to the defined it prevents other things from entering it. And due to what preceded that the purpose of considering suitability in naming is to show the relationship between the primary and secondary meanings, it is not objected that the rasm also prevents the entry of other things because of its specificity to it. And know that this is the convention of the masters of logic. As for others, they apply hadd to the definer generally, whether by essentials or accidents. Keep this in mind, because often confusion occurs due to the lack of distinction between the two conventions.' (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (16) As for the first, because it comprises all the essentials of the defined; and as for the second, because it shares with the first in that in each of them the proximate genus is placed and qualified by a matter specific to the defined. (Muhammad Ali) (17) In it there are other types: the composite of common accident and special property, the composite of it and the differentia, and the composite of differentia and special property. The first is an incomplete rasm, and the latter two are incomplete hadd. If it is said: There is no need to append the special property to the differentia, because the differentia provides distinction and insight into the essential, so there is no benefit in appending the special property to it. We say: The conception of something and insight into it may be through varying ways, some more complete than others. There is no doubt that the insight obtained from both together is stronger than the insight obtained from one alone. So when completeness and strength are desired, there is need to append the special property to the differentia, and likewise in the remaining cases. (Mirza Abd al-Rahim) (18) Because both are deficient relative to the complete hadd and complete rasm. (Muhammad Ali) (19) His statement: 'This is the summary of their speech,' i.e., the restriction of the definer to the four types is the summary of their speech: they said that definition is either by mere essentials or not; the first is either by all of them like the proximate genus and differentia, or by some of them like the proximate differentia alone or with the distant genus. The first is the complete hadd, the second is the incomplete hadd. The second is either by the proximate genus and special property, or by the special property alone or with the distant genus. The first is the complete rasm, the second is the incomplete rasm. This. And it is not hidden what is in it. First, because the restriction of each of the four types to what they mentioned is not exhaustive, because the complete hadd may be obtained by the differentia and the proximate genus, and also by them with the distant differentia, or by them with the distant genus, or by them with

Commentary
This passage addresses two main topics: the condition that the definer be more known than the defined, and the classification of definitions into hadd and rasm. The author first explains that the definer must be known before the defined, otherwise definition would be circular or impossible. He resolves an apparent contradiction with the earlier condition of equality by noting that equality here means equality in obscurity vs. clarity, not in universality vs. particularity. Then, the author classifies definitions: essential definitions (hadd) are those using genus, species, differentia; accidental definitions (rasm) use accidents. The complete hadd uses proximate genus and differentia; incomplete hadd uses only differentia or with distant genus. Complete rasm uses proximate genus and special property; incomplete rasm uses only special property or with distant genus. The author then critiques this fourfold classification, noting that there are other composites (e.g., common accident + special property) which are also possible but not listed. The purpose is to show that the classification is not exhaustive but highlights the main types. The commentary also includes notes on terminology: 'hadd' in logic differs from its common usage, and one must be careful not to confuse them.

Key Terms
أعرف
معرّف
معرف
مساواة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can't the definer and the defined be known simultaneously?
A) Because then one would be prior to the other.
B) Because making one a definer of the other would be arbitrary.
C) Because simultaneous knowledge is impossible.
D) Because the definer must be more specific.

Key Takeaway
The definer must be more known than the defined; otherwise definition fails through simultaneity or posteriority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the definition: 'Salat is an act of worship consisting of specific words and actions.' Identify the definer and the defined. Is the definer more known than the defined? Explain your reasoning.

Classification into Hadd and Rasm
Classification of definitions into four types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify definitions into hadd tamm, hadd naqis, rasm tamm, and rasm naqis based on their components (genus, differentia, special property).

Core Idea
Definitions are either essential (hadd) or accidental (rasm), each complete or incomplete.

This lesson explains the famous classification of definitions into four types: complete essential (hadd tamm), incomplete essential (hadd naqis), complete accidental (rasm tamm), and incomplete accidental (rasm naqis). The passage defines each using the logical components (genus, differentia, special property) and then critiques the classification for not being exhaustive—there are other possible composites. Understanding this classification is crucial for evaluating definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish hadd from rasm
Hadd uses essential parts (genus, differentia); rasm uses accidental parts (common accident, special property).
2
Identify complete vs incomplete
Complete definitions use the proximate genus; incomplete use only the differentia/property or with a distant genus.
3
List the four types
1. Hadd tamm: proximate genus + proximate differentia. 2. Hadd naqis: differentia alone or with distant genus. 3. Rasm tamm: proximate genus + special property. 4. Rasm naqis: special property alone or with distant genus.
4
Examine the critique
The author notes other composites (e.g., common accident + special property, differentia + special property) are possible, so the fourfold classification is not exhaustive.

(محمد علی) (١٧) فيه ان ههنا اقسام اخر وهي المركب من العرض العام والخاصة و المركب منه و من الفصل والمركب من الفصل والخاصة والاول رسم ناقص والاخيران حد ناقص. فان قيل : لاحاجة الى قم الخاصة الى الفصل، لان الفصل يفيد التميز والاطلاع على الذاتى، فلا فايدة في ضم الخاصة اليه. قلنا : تصور أنشىء و الاطلاع عليه قديكون بوجوه متفاوتة بعضها أكمل من بعض ولاشك ان الاطلاع الحاصل منهما معاً اقوى من الاطلاع الحاصل وحده فاذا اريد الاكمال والاقوى، احتيج إلى ضم الخاصة الى الفصل وهكذا الحال فى الباقيين. His statement: 'Then the definer should be more known than the defined.' I say: It should also be known before the definer, otherwise either knowledge of both would be simultaneous or knowledge of the definer would be posterior to knowledge of the defined; and in either case the definer would be a definer of the defined. As for the first, since knowledge of both is simultaneous, making one a definer of the other is not more appropriate than the converse. As for the second, because what is not known cannot be a definer of the known. (Abd al-Rahim) Then he qualified it with that, to avoid misunderstanding of contradiction between this and his statement 'so it is determined that it be equal', because the intended meaning of equality here is equality in obscurity and clarity, while there equality is in universality and particularity; and an indication that equality in knowledge necessitates equality in ignorance, so there is no need to qualify equality with both matters. Thus no objection arises against the author that he should say 'and the equal in knowledge and ignorance' as is the expression of the later scholars. Then he delayed the equal after the more obscure, because the shorter is more deserving of precedence, and the text reversed it for the sake of harmony. (Muhammad Ali) (12) You have known the discussion on that condition, so we say: The definition including a matter specific to the defined is not necessary; rather definition by a common accident is possible if the purpose is to distinguish the thing from some of what is other than it, though consideration of it in the chapter of definition is not commendable. However, what they mentioned of the condition of equality only requires that the common accident not be a definition, not that it not be a part of the definition before the common accident, inasmuch as being a common accident does not provide distinction. For example, something as a common accident does not provide distinction at all, but rather as an accidental special property. (Abd al-Rahim) (13) Because the essentials as mentioned are three: genus, species, and differentia. It has been known previously that the species cannot be a definition because it is more specific, likewise the genus absolutely whether proximate or distant because it is more general, and similarly the distant differentia. Thus the proximate differentia is determined. (Muhammad Ali) (14) Because the accident as preceded is either a common accident or a special property, and it will come that they do not consider the common accident, so the special property is determined. Then it is not hidden that it is permissible that his statement 'necessarily' be a qualifier for each of his statements 'it was a proximate differentia' and 'it was a special property', so reflect. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (15) His statement: 'So according to the first, the definer is called hadd because hadd in language means prevention, and because of its specificity to the defined it prevents other things from entering it. And due to what preceded that the purpose of considering suitability in naming is to show the relationship between the primary and secondary meanings, it is not objected that the rasm also prevents the entry of other things because of its specificity to it. And know that this is the convention of the masters of logic. As for others, they apply hadd to the definer generally, whether by essentials or accidents. Keep this in mind, because often confusion occurs due to the lack of distinction between the two conventions.' (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (16) As for the first, because it comprises all the essentials of the defined; and as for the second, because it shares with the first in that in each of them the proximate genus is placed and qualified by a matter specific to the defined. (Muhammad Ali) (17) In it there are other types: the composite of common accident and special property, the composite of it and the differentia, and the composite of differentia and special property. The first is an incomplete rasm, and the latter two are incomplete hadd. If it is said: There is no need to append the special property to the differentia, because the differentia provides distinction and insight into the essential, so there is no benefit in appending the special property to it. We say: The conception of something and insight into it may be through varying ways, some more complete than others. There is no doubt that the insight obtained from both together is stronger than the insight obtained from one alone. So when completeness and strength are desired, there is need to append the special property to the differentia, and likewise in the remaining cases. (Mirza Abd al-Rahim) (18) Because both are deficient relative to the complete hadd and complete rasm. (Muhammad Ali) (19) His statement: 'This is the summary of their speech,' i.e., the restriction of the definer to the four types is the summary of their speech: they said that definition is either by mere essentials or not; the first is either by all of them like the proximate genus and differentia, or by some of them like the proximate differentia alone or with the distant genus. The first is the complete hadd, the second is the incomplete hadd. The second is either by the proximate genus and special property, or by the special property alone or with the distant genus. The first is the complete rasm, the second is the incomplete rasm. This. And it is not hidden what is in it. First, because the restriction of each of the four types to what they mentioned is not exhaustive, because the complete hadd may be obtained by the differentia and the proximate genus, and also by them with the distant differentia, or by them with the distant genus, or by them with
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage addresses two main topics: the condition that the definer be more known than the defined, and the classification of definitions into hadd and rasm. The author first explains that the definer must be known before the defined, otherwise definition would be circular or impossible. He resolves an apparent contradiction with the earlier condition of equality by noting that equality here means equality in obscurity vs. clarity, not in universality vs. particularity. Then, the author classifies definitions: essential definitions (hadd) are those using genus, species, differentia; accidental definitions (rasm) use accidents. The complete hadd uses proximate genus and differentia; incomplete hadd uses only differentia or with distant genus. Complete rasm uses proximate genus and special property; incomplete rasm uses only special property or with distant genus. The author then critiques this fourfold classification, noting that there are other composites (e.g., common accident + special property) which are also possible but not listed. The purpose is to show that the classification is not exhaustive but highlights the main types. The commentary also includes notes on terminology: 'hadd' in logic differs from its common usage, and one must be careful not to confuse them.

الحد التام
الحد الناقص
الرسم

الرسم الناقص
الخاصة
العرض العام

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT one of the four mentioned types of definition?
A) Hadd tamm: proximate genus + proximate differentia
B) Hadd naqis: differentia alone or with distant genus
C) Rasm tamm: proximate genus + special property
D) Rasm naqis with common accident instead of special property

Key Takeaway
Definitions are classified into four main types: complete/incomplete hadd and rasm, but the classification is not exhaustive; other composites exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Why the Common Accident is Not a Definiens
Author explains why 'arad 'amm is excluded from definiens

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why al-'arad al-'amm (common accident) is excluded as a valid definiens and identify its logical deficiency.

Core Idea
A common accident fails to distinguish—it is universal but not specific.

In defining terms, logicians require that a definiens clearly separate the defined from everything else. The common accident, like 'thing', applies too broadly to provide this separation. This lesson examines that reasoning and connects it to the broader project of achieving precise definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Locate the statement that the common accident 'does not provide distinction' (لا يفيد التمييز). This is the core reason it cannot be a definiens.
2
Understand the reasoning
The text explains that as a common accident, it is too general. For example, 'thing' is said of everything, so it cannot distinguish one thing from another.
Contrast with property (khassa) which is specific to one species.
3
Apply to an Islamic science example
Consider the definition of 'worship' ('ibadah) in Usul al-Fiqh. Including 'commanded' as a definiens is insufficient because many commanded acts (like paying debts) are not worship. 'Commanded' is a common accident.
See other uses in Kalam: 'existent' as a common accident does not define Allah vs. creation.

(ميرزا عبد الرحيم) (١٨) لنقصانهما من الحد التام والرسم التام. ( محمد علي)
On the definiens: that the common accident is not a definiens, not that it is not a part of the definiens. Because considered as a common accident it does not provide distinction. For example, 'thing' as a common accident does not provide distinction at all, but only as a relational property. (Abd al-Rahim) (13) Because the essential (dhatiyat) are three as mentioned: genus, species, and differentia. It was learned earlier that species cannot be a definiens because it is more specific, and likewise genus absolutely, whether proximate or remote, because it is more general, and similarly the remote differentia. So the proximate differentia is determined. (Muhammad Ali) (14) Because the accidental as mentioned is either the common accident or the property, and it will come that they did not consider the common accident, so the property is determined. Then it is not hidden that it is permissible that his statement 'necessarily' be a qualification for each of his statements 'was a proximate differentia' and 'was a property', so reflect. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (15) So his statement: 'Thus, on the first, the definiens is called a definition (hadd)' because 'hadd' in language means prevention, and because it is specific to the defined, it prevents others from entering into it. And given what preceded, that the purpose of considering suitability in naming is to show the suitability between the first and secondary meanings, it is not objected that the descriptive definition (rasm) also prevents others from entering because of its specificity to it. And know that this is the terminology of the masters of logic, but others apply 'hadd' to the definiens universally, whether by essentials or accidents. So keep this in mind, for often confusion occurs due to not distinguishing between the two terminologies. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (16) As for the first, because it comprises all the essentials of the defined; as for the second, because it shares with the first in that each of them places the proximate genus and restricts it by something specific to the defined. (Muhammad Ali) (17) In it, there are other divisions: the composite of common accident and property, the composite of it and the differentia, and the composite of the differentia and property. The first is an incomplete descriptive definition (rasm naqis), and the latter two are incomplete definitions (hadd naqis). So if it is said: There is no need to join the property to the differentia, for the differentia provides distinction and knowledge of the essential, so there is no benefit in joining the property to it. We say: The conception of something and knowledge of it may be through different aspects, some more complete than others, and there is no doubt that the knowledge obtained from both together is stronger than the knowledge obtained from one alone. So when the more complete and stronger is intended, it is necessary to join the property to the differentia, and likewise for the remaining two. (Mirza Abd al-Rahim) (18) Because of their deficiency from the complete definition (hadd tamm) and the complete descriptive definition (rasm tamm). (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory discussion of the definiens (mushtaqq) in logic, specifically focusing on why the common accident ('arad 'amm) cannot serve as a definiens and then defining the terms 'hadd' (definition) and 'rasm' (descriptive definition) with their complete and incomplete forms. The author begins by asserting that the common accident is not a definiens because it does not provide distinction (tamyiz); for example, 'thing' as a common accident does not distinguish anything, only as a relational property does it have some specific meaning. Then, in marginal notes (13) and (14), it is explained that the essential (dhatiyat) are three: genus, species, differentia. Species cannot be a definiens because it is more specific, genus because it is more general, remote differentia also not, so only the proximate differentia works. Similarly, the accidental is either common accident or property (khassa), and since common accident is not considered, property is determined. The note (15) clarifies the term 'hadd': in language it means prevention, and technically it is a definiens that prevents others from entering; this is the terminology of logicians, but others use it broadly for any definiens whether by essentials or accidents, and confusion arises from mixing the two. Note (16) explains why the first two types (hadd tamm and rasm tamm) are considered: one comprises all essentials, the other shares with the first in placing the proximate genus and restricting it. Note (17) expands the classification by adding composite types: common accident+property (rasm naqis), common accident+differentia (hadd naqis), differentia+property (hadd naqis). It also answers an objection that joining property to differentia is redundant, because the differentia alone gives distinction; the reply is that knowledge can be more complete with both, so the stronger is sought. Note (18) simply states that these are deficient relative to the complete forms. The passage thus refines the standard classification of definitions into four types (hadd tamm, hadd naqis, rasm tamm, rasm naqis) by showing there are more possible combinations, especially those involving property plus other items.

Key Terms
العرض العام
التمييز
الخاصة

Checkpoint
Why is the common accident (al-'arad al-'amm) not considered a valid definiens according to this passage?
A) Because it is too specific to the defined thing.
B) Because it does not provide any distinction (tamyiz).
C) Because it is not a universal.
D) Because it is only found in the complete definition (hadd tamm).

Key Takeaway
A common accident is too broad to serve as a definiens because it lacks the necessary distinction (tamyiz).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine the definition of 'sin' (ithm) in kalam: 'an act for which the doer deserves blame'. Identify any common accident in that definition and explain why it would be insufficient.
?
Take the definition of 'body' (jism) in physics: 'a substance that is long, wide, and deep'. Which part is a common accident? Why would a student relying only on that part fail to distinguish a body from a vacuum?

Complete and Incomplete Forms of Hadd and Rasm
Author expands classification of definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify definitions as complete/incomplete hadd or rasm and identify the composite types involving property (khassa).

Core Idea
Definitions are not binary; there are five types, including those combining property with differentia.

The passage expands the basic two-type classification (hadd and rasm) by introducing three composite types: common accident + property (rasm naqis), common accident + differentia (hadd naqis), and differentia + property (hadd naqis). This lesson guides you through this expansion and its justification.

Guided Path
1
Recall the base classification
From earlier lessons, a hadd uses essential parts (genus and differentia), a rasm uses accidental parts (genus and property). The passage begins from this.
Base text: definiens is either hadd (essential) or rasm (accidental).
2
Identify the expansions
Note (17) adds three composites: (1) common accident + property, (2) common accident + differentia, (3) differentia + property. The first is rasm naqis; the latter two are hadd naqis.
Why? Because they combine essential and accidental, or two accidentals.
3
Understand the rebuttal
An objection says adding property to differentia is redundant. The answer: knowledge from two sources is stronger; so composite gives a more complete conception.
This shows the value of completeness in definitions.
4
Apply to an Islamic science example
Define 'human' in kalam: a complete hadd would be 'rational animal' (genus + differentia). A composite hadd naqis could be 'rational animal that laughs' (differentia + property). A rasm tamm could be 'animal that laughs' (genus + property).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في المعرف أن لا يكون العرض العام معرفاً لا أن لا يكون جزء من المعرف قبل العرض العام من حيث أنه عرض عام لا يفيد التمييز فان الشيء مثلاً من حيث أنه عرض عام لا يفيد التمييز أصلاً بل من حيث أنه خاصة إضافية. (عبد الرحيم) (١٣) لأن الذاتيات كما مر ثلاثة: الجنس و النوع و الفصل وقد علم فيما سبق أن النوع لا يكون معرفاً لأنه أخص وكذلك الجنس مطلقا قريباً كان أو بعيدا لأنه أعم وهكذا الفصل البعيد فتعين الفصل القريب. (محمد علي) (١٤) لأن العرضي كما تقدم إما العرض العام أو الخاصة و سيجيء أنهم لم يعتبروا بالعرض العام فتعين الخاصة. : ثم لا يخفى أنه يجوز أن يكون قوله : « لا محالة» قيداً لكل من قوله : كان فصلاً قريباً وقوله: «كان خاصة» فتأمل. (ميرزا محمد علي) (١٥) قوله فعلى الأول المعرف يسمى حداً لأن الحد في اللغة المنع وهو لاختصاصه بالمحدود يمنع الأغيار عن الدخول فيه وبما سبق من أن الغرض من اعتبار المناسبة في التسمية بيان المناسبة بين المعنى الأولى والثانوي، لا يرد أن الرسم أيضاً مانع من دخول الأغيار لاختصاصه به و اعلم أن هذا اصطلاح أرباب المنطق وأما غيرهم فيطلقون الحد على المعرف أعم من أن يكون بالذاتيات والعرضيات وليكن هذا على ذكر منك فكثيراً ما يقع الخلط بسبب عدم الفرق بين الاصطلاحين. (ميرزا محمد علي) (١٦) أما الأول فلاته تمام ذاتيات المعرف، و أما الثاني فلاشتراكه الأول من حيث أن في كل واحد منهما وضع الجنس القريب وقيد بأمر يختص بالمعرف. (محمد علي) (١٧) فيه أن ههنا أقسام أخر وهي المركب من العرض العام والخاصة و المركب منه و من الفصل والمركب من الفصل والخاصة والأول رسم ناقص والآخران حد ناقص. فان قيل : لا حاجة إلى ضم الخاصة إلى الفصل، لأن الفصل يفيد التميز والاطلاع على الذاتي، فلا فائدة في ضم الخاصة إليه. قلنا : تصور أنشئ و الاطلاع عليه قد يكون بوجوه متفاوتة بعضها أكمل من بعض ولا شك أن الاطلاع الحاصل منهما معاً أقوى من الاطلاع الحاصل وحده فإذا أريد الإكمال والأقوى، احتيج إلى ضم الخاصة إلى الفصل وهكذا الحال في الباقيين. (ميرزا عبد الرحيم) (١٨) لنقصانهما من الحد التام والرسم التام. ( محمد علي)
On the definiens: that the common accident is not a definiens, not that it is not a part of the definiens. Because considered as a common accident it does not provide distinction. For example, 'thing' as a common accident does not provide distinction at all, but only as a relational property. (Abd al-Rahim) (13) Because the essential (dhatiyat) are three as mentioned: genus, species, and differentia. It was learned earlier that species cannot be a definiens because it is more specific, and likewise genus absolutely, whether proximate or remote, because it is more general, and similarly the remote differentia. So the proximate differentia is determined. (Muhammad Ali) (14) Because the accidental as mentioned is either the common accident or the property, and it will come that they did not consider the common accident, so the property is determined. Then it is not hidden that it is permissible that his statement 'necessarily' be a qualification for each of his statements 'was a proximate differentia' and 'was a property', so reflect. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (15) So his statement: 'Thus, on the first, the definiens is called a definition (hadd)' because 'hadd' in language means prevention, and because it is specific to the defined, it prevents others from entering into it. And given what preceded, that the purpose of considering suitability in naming is to show the suitability between the first and secondary meanings, it is not objected that the descriptive definition (rasm) also prevents others from entering because of its specificity to it. And know that this is the terminology of the masters of logic, but others apply 'hadd' to the definiens universally, whether by essentials or accidents. So keep this in mind, for often confusion occurs due to not distinguishing between the two terminologies. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (16) As for the first, because it comprises all the essentials of the defined; as for the second, because it shares with the first in that each of them places the proximate genus and restricts it by something specific to the defined. (Muhammad Ali) (17) In it, there are other divisions: the composite of common accident and property, the composite of it and the differentia, and the composite of the differentia and property. The first is an incomplete descriptive definition (rasm naqis), and the latter two are incomplete definitions (hadd naqis). So if it is said: There is no need to join the property to the differentia, for the differentia provides distinction and knowledge of the essential, so there is no benefit in joining the property to it. We say: The conception of something and knowledge of it may be through different aspects, some more complete than others, and there is no doubt that the knowledge obtained from both together is stronger than the knowledge obtained from one alone. So when the more complete and stronger is intended, it is necessary to join the property to the differentia, and likewise for the remaining two. (Mirza Abd al-Rahim) (18) Because of their deficiency from the complete definition (hadd tamm) and the complete descriptive definition (rasm tamm). (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory discussion of the definiens (mushtaqq) in logic, specifically focusing on why the common accident ('arad 'amm) cannot serve as a definiens and then defining the terms 'hadd' (definition) and 'rasm' (descriptive definition) with their complete and incomplete forms. The author begins by asserting that the common accident is not a definiens because it does not provide distinction (tamyiz); for example, 'thing' as a common accident does not distinguish anything, only as a relational property does it have some specific meaning. Then, in marginal notes (13) and (14), it is explained that the essential (dhatiyat) are three: genus, species, differentia. Species cannot be a definiens because it is more specific, genus because it is more general, remote differentia also not, so only the proximate differentia works. Similarly, the accidental is either common accident or property (khassa), and since common accident is not considered, property is determined. The note (15) clarifies the term 'hadd': in language it means prevention, and technically it is a definiens that prevents others from entering; this is the terminology of logicians, but others use it broadly for any definiens whether by essentials or accidents, and confusion arises from mixing the two. Note (16) explains why the first two types (hadd tamm and rasm tamm) are considered: one comprises all essentials, the other shares with the first in placing the proximate genus and restricting it. Note (17) expands the classification by adding composite types: common accident+property (rasm naqis), common accident+differentia (hadd naqis), differentia+property (hadd naqis). It also answers an objection that joining property to differentia is redundant, because the differentia alone gives distinction; the reply is that knowledge can be more complete with both, so the stronger is sought. Note (18) simply states that these are deficient relative to the complete forms. The passage thus refines the standard classification of definitions into four types (hadd tamm, hadd naqis, rasm tamm, rasm naqis) by showing there are more possible combinations, especially those involving property plus other items.

Key Terms
الحد التام
الحد الناقص
الرسم التام
الرسم الناقص

A) Hadd tamm
B) Rasm tamm
C) Hadd naqis
D) Rasm naqis

Key Takeaway
Definitions can be complete or incomplete; composite types that mix essential and accidental parts provide stronger knowledge but are still deficient.

Hadd and Rasm

Logician vs. Non-Logician Terminology of 'Hadd'
Author warns about terminology confusion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the logician's technical use of 'hadd' (essential definition) from the broader non-logician usage.

Core Idea
One word, two meanings: logicians restrict 'hadd' to essential definitions; others use it for any definiens.

In note (15), the author warns that non-logicians (like grammarians) apply the term 'hadd' to any definiens, whether essential or accidental. This lesson clarifies the confusion and shows why precision matters when reading Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terminologies
Logicians define 'hadd' as a definiens composed of essential parts (genus and differentia) that prevents others from entering. Non-logicians use it for any definiens, including those with accidents.
2
Understand the reason for confusion
Because both are called 'hadd', a reader may mistakenly think a rasm is an essential definition. The author says 'كثيراً ما يقع الخلط' (often confusion occurs).
3
Apply to an example from grammar
In Arabic grammar, 'kalima' is defined as 'a meaningful utterance'—this is a rasm (uses accidents like 'meaningful'), but grammarians call it a hadd. A logician would object that it does not give the essence.
Similarly, in usul, definitions of 'amr' (command) often mix essential and accidental features.
4
Practice distinguishing
When you encounter 'hadd' in a text, check whether the author is a logician or a specialist in another field. If the definiens uses accidentals, it is likely a non-logician's hadd.

The Standard Four-Type Classification of Definitions
Summary of the standard four-type classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the four standard definition types (hadd tamm, hadd naqis, rasm tamm, rasm naqis) and specify their components.

Core Idea
Four types of definitions stem from whether you include all essentials, some essentials, or non-essentials.

In classical logic, definitions are classified into four types based on whether they use all essential parts, some essential parts, or non-essential parts. This lesson introduces that classification as a foundation for understanding more complex critiques.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two main branches
Definitions are either by essentials (hadd) or by non-essentials (rasm). Identify which branch corresponds to which.
2
Focus on hadd: complete vs incomplete
If all essentials are included, it's hadd tamm (proximate genus + proximate differentia). If only some (e.g., differentia alone), it's hadd naqis.
3
Focus on rasm: complete vs incomplete
If it includes proximate genus and property, it's rasm tamm. If only property or with remote genus, it's rasm naqis.
4
Memorize the examples
The text gives examples: hadd tamm = jins qareeb + fasl qareeb; hadd naqis = fasl qareeb alone or with jins ba‘eed; rasm tamm = jins qareeb + khassa; rasm naqis = khassa alone or with jins ba‘eed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(١٩) قوله هذا محصل كلامهم أي انحصار المعرف في الأقسام الأربعة محصل كلامهم حيث قالوا إن التعريف إما بمجرد الذاتيات أولاً والأول إما أن يكون بجميعها كالجنس و الفصل القريبين أو ببعضها كالفصل القريب خاصة أو هو مع الجنس البعيد، الأول هو الحد التام، الثاني هو: الناقص. والثاني إما أن يكون بالجنس القريب والخاصة أولاً بل بالخاصة وحدها أو مع الجنس البعيد. الأول هو الرسم التام والثاني هو الرسم الناقص هذا. ولا يخفى ما فيه، أما أولاً، لعدم انحصار كل من الأقسام الأربعة بما ذكروه ضرورة أن الحد التام كما يحصل بالفصل والجنس القريبين فقد يحصل بهما مع الفصل البعيد و بهما مع الجنس البعيد و بهما مع الخاصة وبهما مع العرض العام وغير ذلك والحد الناقص كما يحصل بالفصل القريب خاصة وبه مع الجنس البعيد فكذا يحصل بالفصل القريب و الخاصة وبه مع العرض العام وبه مع الفصل البعيد و به مع الجنس البعيد و الخاصة وبه مع الجنس البعيد والعرض العام و به مع الجنس البعيد والفصل البعيد وغير ذلك و هكذا حال الرسم التام والناقص. والتفصيل: إن الكليات خمسة ومع ملاحظة كل من قسمي الجنس و الفصل تصير سبعة، فح نقول : إن المعرف إما بسيط أولا وعلى الثاني إما ثنائي أو ثلاثي أو رباعي أو خماسي أو سداسي أو سباعي سبع صور صحيحها اثنان و البواقي غير صحيح إما للعموم أو للخصوص. والثنائي تسع و أربعون صورة حاصلة من ملاحظة السبعة مع السبعة بعضها غير صحيح للعموم أو للخصوص أو لتقدم الأخص على الأعم خاصة أو مع واحد من الأولين وبعضها يرجع إلى البسائط و ترسم لها جدولاً يسهل تمييز الصحاح عن الغير والمعتبر منها عن الغير و يعلم منه حال البسائط أيضاً و هو هذا:
(19) His statement: 'This is the gist of their speech,' i.e., that the limitation of the definiens to four divisions is the gist of their speech when they said: 'The definition is either by essentials alone or not. The first is either by all of them, like the proximate genus and differentia, or by some of them, like the proximate differentia alone, or it is with the remote genus. The first is the complete definition (hadd tamm), the second is the incomplete definition (hadd naqis). And the second is either by the proximate genus and property, or by the property alone, or with the remote genus. The first is the complete descriptive definition (rasm tamm), and the second is the incomplete descriptive definition (rasm naqis). This is [their claim]. And it is not hidden what is in it. Firstly, because each of the four divisions is not limited to what they mentioned, necessarily: the complete definition (hadd tamm) can be obtained by the differentia and the proximate genus, and it can be obtained by them together with the remote differentia, and by them together with the remote genus, and by them together with the property, and by them together with the common accident, and so on. And the incomplete definition (hadd naqis) can be obtained by the proximate differentia alone, and by it with the remote genus, likewise it can be obtained by the proximate differentia and the property, and by it with the common accident, and by it with the remote differentia, and by it with the remote genus and the property, and by it with the remote genus and the common accident, and by it with the remote genus and the remote differentia, and so on. And similarly for the complete and incomplete descriptive definitions (rasm tamm and naqis). The detail: The universals are five, and when considering each of the two divisions of genus and differentia, they become seven. So we say: The definiens is either simple or not, and the second is either binary, ternary, quaternary, quinary, senary, or septenary—seven forms, two of which are correct, and the rest are incorrect due to generality or specificity. And the binary has forty-nine forms resulting from considering the seven with the seven. Some of them are incorrect due to generality or specificity, or due to the precedence of the more specific over the more general specifically, or with one of the first two. And some of them revert to the simple forms. And we draw a table for it that facilitates distinguishing the correct from the incorrect, and the accepted from the others, and from it the state of the simple forms is also known. And it is this:
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hāshiya) of the standard classification of definitions into four types (hadd tamm, hadd nāqis, rasm tamm, rasm nāqis) as presented in earlier logical texts. The author (likely Mīrzā ʿAbd al-Raḥīm or another commentator) argues that the limitation is not exhaustive. He first summarizes the standard claim: definitions are either by essentials alone or not; if by essentials, either by all (genus+proximate differentia) = hadd tamm, or by some (proximate differentia alone, or with remote genus) = hadd nāqis; if not by essentials, either by genus+property = rasm tamm, or by property alone or with remote genus = rasm nāqis. Then he critiques: each of these four types can have many more combinations. For example, hadd tamm can be obtained by (proximate differentia + proximate genus) plus remote differentia, or plus remote genus, or plus property, or plus common accident, etc. Hadd nāqis can be by proximate differentia alone, or with remote genus, or with property, or with common accident, or with remote differentia, etc. Similarly for rasm. He then provides a systematic enumeration: The universals are five (jins, naw', fasl, khāṣṣa, ʿaraḍ 'āmm) but since genus is divided into proximate and remote, and differentia into proximate and remote, the effective categories become seven: (1) proximate genus, (2) remote genus, (3) species, (4) proximate differentia, (5) remote differentia, (6) common accident, (7) property. The definiens can have 1 to 7 components. Simple (one component) are correct only for species (which is not a definiens) or perhaps property? Actually, the text says out of the seven simple forms, only two are correct (probably species and property? but species is not a definiens—this point needs clarity). Then binary combinations: 7x7 = 49 possible pairs, many invalid due to generality (e.g., two genera) or specificity (e.g., two species). The author mentions drawing a table to distinguish. This passage thus refines the logical taxonomy of definitions by showing that the four-type scheme is incomplete, and offers a combinatorial enumeration.

Critique of the Four-Type Classification and the Combinatorial Method
Critique of the four-type limitation and introduction of the combinatorial enumeration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the seven universal categories and use them to generate all possible definition combinations, identifying valid and invalid ones based on generality and specificity.

Core Idea
Definitions are not limited to four types; a combinatorial approach using seven universal categories yields many possibilities.

The author of this hashya argues that the classical four-type classification is incomplete. By splitting genus and differentia into proximate and remote, we get seven categories. Then, definitions can be formed by combining one, two, or more of these categories, leading to many valid forms beyond the traditional four.

Guided Path
1
Understand the seven categories
The five universals become seven by splitting genus and differentia into proximate and remote. List them: (1) proximate genus, (2) remote genus, (3) species, (4) proximate differentia, (5) remote differentia, (6) property, (7) common accident.
The passage says 'the universals are five, and when considering each of the two divisions of genus and differentia, they become seven.'
2
Recognize simple (basit) definitions
A simple definiens contains only one category. The text says out of seven simple forms, only two are correct – likely species and property? But note: species alone is problematic. The key is that simple definitions are rare.
The passage states: 'seven forms, two of which are correct and the rest incorrect due to generality or specificity.' The exact two correct ones are not explicitly named here.
3
Explore binary combinations
The author mentions 49 possible pairs (7x7). Many are invalid due to generality (e.g., two genera), specificity (e.g., two species), or ordering (more specific before more general). Identify valid pairs: e.g., proximate genus + proximate differentia (hadd tamm), proximate genus + property (rasm tamm).
Use the conditions of being neither too general nor too specific.
4
Apply validity conditions
A definition must be neither too general (includes non-instances) nor too specific (excludes some instances). Also, the more specific should not precede the more general. Apply these to evaluate any combination.
The passage mentions 'due to generality or specificity' and 'due to the precedence of the more specific over the more general'.

(19) His statement: 'This is the gist of their speech,' i.e., that the limitation of the definiens to four divisions is the gist of their speech when they said: 'The definition is either by essentials alone or not. The first is either by all of them, like the proximate genus and differentia, or by some of them, like the proximate differentia alone, or it is with the remote genus. The first is the complete definition (hadd tamm), the second is the incomplete definition (hadd naqis). And the second is either by the proximate genus and property, or by the property alone, or with the remote genus. The first is the complete descriptive definition (rasm tamm), and the second is the incomplete descriptive definition (rasm naqis). This is [their claim]. And it is not hidden what is in it. Firstly, because each of the four divisions is not limited to what they mentioned, necessarily: the complete definition (hadd tamm) can be obtained by the differentia and the proximate genus, and it can be obtained by them together with the remote differentia, and by them together with the remote genus, and by them together with the property, and by them together with the common accident, and so on. And the incomplete definition (hadd naqis) can be obtained by the proximate differentia alone, and by it with the remote genus, likewise it can be obtained by the proximate differentia and the property, and by it with the common accident, and by it with the remote differentia, and by it with the remote genus and the property, and by it with the remote genus and the common accident, and by it with the remote genus and the remote differentia, and so on. And similarly for the complete and incomplete descriptive definitions (rasm tamm and naqis). The detail: The universals are five, and when considering each of the two divisions of genus and differentia, they become seven. So we say: The definiens is either simple or not, and the second is either binary, ternary, quaternary, quinary, senary, or septenary—seven forms, two of which are correct, and the rest are incorrect due to generality or specificity. And the binary has forty-nine forms resulting from considering the seven with the seven. Some of them are incorrect due to generality or specificity, or due to the precedence of the more specific over the more general specifically, or with one of the first two. And some of them revert to the simple forms. And we draw a table for it that facilitates distinguishing the correct from the incorrect, and the accepted from the others, and from it the state of the simple forms is also known. And it is this:

Identifying Valid Combinations of the Seven Universals
Enumerating valid and invalid combinations of the seven universals in definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which binary combinations of the seven categorical terms constitute a valid definition and determine the reason for invalidity (general or specific).

Core Idea
Out of 49 binary combinations of the seven universals, only two produce correct definitions.

The five universals—genus, species, differentia, property, and common accident—are subdivided into seven by distinguishing proximate and distant genus and differentia. From these seven terms, definitions can be composed in various combinations, but most are logically invalid. This lesson shows how to identify the correct ones and diagnose why others fail, using the criteria of generality and specificity.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the seven categorical terms
List the seven terms derived from the five universals: proximate genus, distant genus, proximate differentia, distant differentia, species, property (special characteristic), and common accident. These are the building blocks of definitions in this analysis.
The five universals are standard; splitting genus and differentia into proximate and distant is specific to this combinatorial discussion.
2
Understand validity criteria for definitions
A valid definition must either reveal the essence (hadd) or distinguish the thing (rasm). It must not be too general (failing to exclude others) or too specific (including extraneous conditions). The presence of species as a part causes specificity; lack of both proximate differentia and property causes generality.
The author states that correct definitions are only two; rest are invalid due to generality, specificity, or ordering.
3
Analyze binary combinations
Consider all 49 possible pairs of the seven terms. Apply the validity criteria: exclude any pair that includes species (specificity) or excludes both proximate differentia and property (generality). Also exclude if the more specific precedes the more general without justification.
Only two pairs survive: (distant genus + proximate differentia) for an incomplete hadd, and (property + common accident) for a complete rasm.
4
Generalize to higher-order combinations
The same principles apply to ternary and higher compositions. The author calculates counts (e.g., 336 ternary, 3360 quaternary) but the key is that only two composite definitions are correct overall, regardless of length.
The combinatorial numbers are derived, but the logical lesson is about the conditions for correctness, not the arithmetic.

And the special characteristic, and with both of them along with the common accident and so on. And the incomplete definition is obtained by the proximate differentia alone, and with it along with the distant genus; similarly it is obtained by the proximate differentia and the special characteristic, and with it along with the common accident, and with it along with the distant differentia, and with it along with the distant genus and the special characteristic, and with it along with the distant genus and the common accident, and with it along with the distant genus and the distant differentia, and so on. And such is the case for complete and incomplete description. The detail: the universals are five, and with the observation of each of the two divisions of genus and differentia they become seven. So we say: the definiens is either simple or not, and on the second [alternative] it is either binary, ternary, quaternary, quinary, senary, or septenary; correct ones are two, and the rest are incorrect, either due to generality or specificity. The binary [consists of] forty-nine forms resulting from observing the seven with the seven; some of them are incorrect due to generality, or specificity, or the precedence of the more specific over the more general alone or together with one of the first two, and some of them reduce to simple ones. We draw a table for it to easily distinguish the correct from the incorrect and the considered from the unconsidered, and from it the state of the simple ones is also known. And it is this: [corrupted table]. The ternary [consists of] three hundred and thirty-six forms. For the ternary composition among the seven rises to fifty-six. Because if we compose the proximate and distant genus and the proximate differentia, for example, in one composition, and the distant differentia and the common accident in another composition, there are two forms. And if we replace every part of the parts of one composition with every part of the parts of the other, eighteen forms are obtained; with the first two they become twenty. And if we replace every part of the parts of one composition with the species, for example, six forms are obtained. And if we replace every part of the last two parts—other than the species—of these six forms that contain the species, with each one of the remaining three, thirty-six forms are obtained; with the previous twenty they become fifty-six. The possibilities in each composition according to the precedence of some over others are six. The product of observing the six with the fifty-six is three hundred and thirty-six, which is what was sought. The quaternary [consists of] three thousand three hundred and sixty forms. For the quaternary composition among the seven rises to one hundred and forty. Because if one wants to compose from the seven two compositions that do not share parts as much as possible, they inevitably share one part that is undetermined among the seven. So these are fourteen forms; for each of the two compositions there are seven forms. And if we replace each part of the three unshared parts of the images of each of the supposed seven compositions with each part of the three unshared parts of the images of the other seven compositions, one hundred and twenty-six forms are obtained, as is not hidden, which with the fourteen make one hundred and forty, which is what was sought. The possibilities in each of these compositions are twenty-four. The product of observing the number of compositions with the number of possibilities is three thousand three hundred and sixty. The quinary [consists of] five thousand four hundred and forty? [sic] forms. For the quinary composition among the seven [consists of] four hundred and fifty-four? [sic] necessarily. Because if two compositions are composed from the seven that do not share parts as much as possible, they must share three parts from the seven that are undetermined among fifty-six? forms, as was shown in the ternary composition. So these are one hundred and twelve forms; for each of these two compositions there are fifty-six forms. And if we replace each of the two unshared parts from each of the images of one composition with each of the two unshared parts from the images of the other composition, it reaches four hundred and fifty-four? The supposed possibilities in each of these compositions are one hundred and twenty. The product of observing the number of compositions with the number of possibilities is five thousand four hundred and forty? which is what was sought. And each of the senary and the septenary [consists of] five thousand and forty forms. As for the first: the senary composition among the seven is seven, as is apparent, and the possibilities in each of them are seven hundred and twenty. The product of observing the number of possibilities with the number of compositions is five thousand and forty, which is what was sought. As for the second: although it has one form, the possibilities in it rise to what was mentioned, and since the one has no effect in multiplication, the number of possibilities becomes the number of compositions. And the rule for possibilities in compositions is that the number of possibilities obtained in the previous [case] is multiplied by the number of parts of the subsequent [case]; the product is the possibilities of the subsequent. Then some of them are correct and some are incorrect: due to specification, as when the species is one of the parts; or due to generality, as when neither the special characteristic nor the proximate differentia is present in it; or due to the precedence of the more specific over the more general. That is it. It cannot be said that the purpose of definition is either knowledge of the essence or distinction from everything else, and this is obtained...
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage is a detailed combinatorial analysis of definitions and descriptions (حد and رسم) based on the seven subdivisions of the five universals. The author begins by illustrating how an incomplete definition (حد ناقص) can be formed from various combinations: e.g., from the proximate differentia alone, or the proximate differentia with the distant genus, etc. Similarly for complete and incomplete descriptions (رسم تام وناقص). He then states that the universals are five (الجنس, النوع, الفصل, الخاصة, العرض العام), but when genus is divided into proximate and distant, and differentia into proximate and distant, they become seven. From these seven, a definiens is either simple (one part) or composite. Composite definitions range from binary to septenary. Only two composite definitions are correct, while the rest are invalid due to generality (عموم) or specificity (خصوص). For binary combinations, there are 49 possible pairings of the seven, but many are invalid due to various reasons including ordering. The author then proposes a table to distinguish correct from incorrect, but the OCR has mangled it. Next, he calculates the number of ternary combinations (336), quaternary (3360), quinary (5440? the text has 'سي خمسة آلاف و اربع مأت' which likely means 5040? but arithmetic shows 5040? Actually 120*42=5040, but he says 5440? Possibly a scribal error), senary and septenary (5040 each). He explains the derivation: for ternary, the number of composition forms is 56, each with 6 permutations, yielding 336. For quaternary, 140 composition forms each with 24 permutations gives 3360. For quinary, 448? composition forms? The text is unclear. Then he states that senary has 7 composition forms with 720 permutations = 5040; septenary has 1 composition form with 5040 permutations = 5040. Finally, he gives the rule: multiply the number of permutations of the previous case by the number of parts of the next case. Then he reiterates the reasons for invalidity: if the species is a part (invalid due to specificity), if neither الخاصة nor الفصل القريب is present (invalid due to generality), or if a more specific precedes a more general. This concludes the enumeration.

Calculating Possible Definitions from Seven Parts
Author enumerates the combinatorial possibilities for definitions of three to seven parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the number of possible definitions from a fixed set of definitional parts using combinatorial reasoning.

Core Idea
The number of possible definitions is not infinite—it can be precisely calculated.

In this passage, the author demonstrates how to systematically count all possible definitions that can be formed from seven definitional parts: genus, differentia, species, accident, etc. This is not just a mathematical exercise; it reveals that definitions in logic are structured and finite. Understanding this combinatorial method helps us appreciate why certain definitions are chosen and how completeness is evaluated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the seven definitional parts
The passage works with seven parts: proximate genus, remote genus, proximate differentia, remote differentia, general accident, species, and property (or specific accident). These are the building blocks for definitions.
These parts are standard in classical logic; the exact list may vary slightly across textbooks.
2
Understand the calculation for three-part definitions
The author first determines the number of distinct three-part compositions (56) by considering possible selections and replacements of parts. Then, for each composition, there are 6 possible orderings (permutations), giving 56 × 6 = 336 total possibilities.
The 56 comes from pairwise combinations and replacements; the 6 is 3!.
3
Extend the method to four-part and five-part definitions
For four-part definitions, the number of compositions is 140, each with 24 orderings (4!), giving 140 × 24 = 3,360. For five-part, compositions are 450 (or 454 depending on reading), each with 120 orderings (5!), yielding 5,400 (or 5,? ) — the text says 5,400.
The exact number for five-part may need verification due to OCR, but the method is clear.
4
Apply the rule for six-part and seven-part definitions
Six-part compositions are 7 (choose 6 out of 7), each with 720 orderings (6!), giving 7 × 720 = 5,040. Seven-part has only 1 composition (all 7 parts), but 5,040 orderings (7!), so the total is also 5,040 because multiplying by 1 does nothing.
This illustrates that the number of orderings dominates for larger compositions.
