Three Invalidating Conditions for Definitions
Three invalidity conditions for definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the three conditions that make a definition composition invalid.

Core Idea
A valid definition must be neither too specific, too general, nor improperly ordered.

In this passage, the author identifies three specific conditions that render a definition invalid. Understanding these conditions will help you evaluate whether a definition in Islamic sciences – such as definitions of legal terms or theological concepts – is correctly formed. We will walk through each condition and see why it fails.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three invalidity conditions
The author lists three reasons: (1) 'for specificity' when the species is one of the parts, (2) 'for generality' when neither the property (khāṣṣa) nor the proximate differentia (faṣl qarīb) is included, (3) 'due to priority of the more specific over the more general'.
These correspond to definitions being too narrow, too broad, or improperly ordered.
2
Analyze the 'specificity' condition
If the species itself is used as a part of the definition, the definition becomes circular or excludes other species under the same genus, making it invalidly narrow.
Example: defining 'human' as 'rational animal' but including 'human' itself as a component would be invalid.
3
Analyze the 'generality' condition
A definition must contain either a property (khāṣṣa) or a proximate differentia (faṣl qarīb) to distinguish the defined term from others. Lacking both makes the definition too broad and inclusive of things not intended.
For instance, defining 'human' as 'animal' alone is too general because it includes all animals.
4
Analyze the 'ordering' condition
In a definition, more general parts should come before more specific ones. Placing the more specific first disrupts the logical hierarchy and invalidates the definition.
Example: in 'rational animal', 'animal' (general) should precede 'rational' (specific).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Then indeed some of them are correct and some are incorrect: for specificity, as when the species is one of the parts, or for generality, as when there is not in it one of the property and the proximate differentia, or due to the priority of the more specific over the more general. That is it.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage identifies three conditions under which a definitional composition is invalid. First, if the species is one of the parts, the definition becomes circular or too narrow (specific). Second, if neither the property (khassa) nor the proximate differentia (fasl qarib) is included, the definition lacks proper distinguishing features and is too general. Third, if a more specific part is given priority over a more general part, the order of precedence is violated. These conditions ensure that a definition properly distinguishes the defined term from others.

Why Equal Parts Cannot Define a Quiddity
Author critiques definitions from equal parts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why a definition composed of two equal parts (mutasāwiyān) fails to be a valid hadd or rasm.

Core Idea
Definitions need a differentia or property; equal parts fulfill neither.

This passage addresses a key limitation in definition theory: when a quiddity is composed of two coextensive parts (mutasāwiyān) and we define it by one of them, that part cannot serve as a differentia (fasl) nor as a property (khāṣṣa). The author explains that differentiae only exist in hierarchical genus-differentia compositions, and properties are accidental. Thus the definition is neither hadd nor rasm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Claim
The author asserts that if a quiddity is composed of two equal parts (mutasāwiyān) and we define it by one, the definition is not a valid hadd or rasm. The intermediate (wāsiṭa) is established, meaning the definition fails to capture the essence.
2
Understand Why the Part Is Not a Differentia
The part used cannot be a differentia (fasl) because differentiae only exist in quiddities that are composed of genera and differentiae in a hierarchical relation. In equal parts, there is no genus-differentia hierarchy; they are coextensive.
The author distinguishes proximate and remote differentiae, but both require a genus-differentia structure.
3
Understand Why the Part Is Not a Property
Even if the part is an essential attribute (dhātī), it is not a property (khāṣṣa) because properties are accidental (not essential). Since the part is essential, it cannot be a property, and rasm requires a property.
Property (khāṣṣa) is a non-essential distinctive attribute, whereas equal parts are essential constituents of the quiddity.
4
Conclude: Neither Hadd nor Rasm
For a definition to be a hadd, it must include a proximate differentia; for a rasm, it must include a property. Since neither condition is met, the definition is invalid. The author points to an earlier remark about distinguishing associates in a genus for further clarification.
The core of the argument: the part used is not a differentia (no hierarchy) and not a property (essential, not accidental).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع و اما ثانياً: فلانه اذا جوزنا تركب الماهية من الأمرين المتساوبين او الامور المتساوية وعرفنا ها بواحد منهما أو منها فهذا التعريف لا يكون حداً و لارسماً وتثبت الواسطة لان ذلك الواحد ليس بفصل قريب ولاخاصة . اما الاول : فلان الفصل القريب والبعيد كما سبق اليه الاشارة فيما سبق انما يكونان في الماهيات المركبة من الاجناس و الفصول لا الماهيات المركبة من الامرين المتساويين أو الامور المتساوية. و اما الثاني: فلان ذلك الواحد من الذاتيات والخاصة ليست بذاتية فاذا لم يكن فصلاً ولا خاصة لم يكن حداً ولا رسماً، لان الحد هو التعريف بالفصل القريب والرسم هو التعريف بالخاصة على ما ذكره المصنف فثبتت الواسطة و هو المطلوب و قد سبق منا عند قول المصنف عند قول المصنف فان ميزه عن المشاركات في الجنس القريب فقريب أو البعيد بعيد ما ينفعك هنا فراجع و تأمل

Commentary
This passage addresses a second point: if a quiddity is composed of two equal things (i.e., coextensive and non-hierarchical parts) and we define it by just one of them, that definition cannot be a valid ḥadd or rasm. The reason is that the part used would not be a proximate differentia (since differentiae only exist in hierarchical compositions) nor a property (since property is accidental). Since ḥadd must include a differentia and rasm must include a property, the definition fails. The author also points to an earlier passage about distinguishing associates in a genus as relevant.

Two Objections Against Restricting the Purpose of Definition
Marginal note criticizing the restriction of definition's purpose.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate the two objections against restricting the purpose of definition to essence and distinction.

Core Idea
The purpose of definition is not limited to essence; accidental properties can also be a goal.

In classical logic, some argued that the only purposes of definition are to know the essence (dhāt) and to distinguish (tamyīz). This marginal note from a hashia challenges that restriction with two incisive points. We will unpack each objection and see how they refine our understanding of what definitions can achieve.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
Find the claim being criticized: that the purpose of definition is exclusively knowing the essence and distinguishing from others.
This is the position the commentator rejects.
2
Extract the first objection
The commentator says the restriction is not conceded; it is permissible that the purpose includes knowledge of accidental properties (al-ʿaraḍī).
This broadens the scope beyond essential knowledge.
3
Extract the second objection
Even if the restriction is granted, it only requires that a general accident alone cannot serve as a complete definition, not that it cannot be a part of a definition.
This shows the original argument commits a logical leap.
4
Assess the logical impact
These objections together show that the argument against using definitions that include accidental properties (like genus plus accident) is invalid.
The commentator refines the conditions for a valid definition.

قوله قالوا الغرض من التعريف ... فيه نظر، اما اولاً ، فلعدم تسليم انحصار الغرض فيهما و اما ان بل يجوز ان يكون الغرض الاطلاع على العرضي ايضاً. و اما ثانياً، فلانه على تقدير تسليمه انما يقتضى ان لا يكون العرض العام وحده معرفاً لا يكون جزء من المعرف فلا
His statement: 'They said the purpose of definition...' is open to scrutiny. Firstly, because the restriction of the purpose to them is not conceded. But rather, it is permissible that the purpose is also knowledge of the accidental (ʿaraḍī). And secondly, because if we concede it, it only requires that the general accident alone should not be a definer, not that it should not be a part of the definer. So [the argument fails].
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This marginal note critiques the earlier objection about the purpose of definition. The commentator (Mirza Muhammad Ali) raises two points. First, he does not accept that the purpose is limited to knowing the essence or distinguishing from others; it could also be to know accidental properties. Second, even if one concedes that limitation, it would only imply that a general accident alone cannot serve as a complete definition, not that it cannot be a part of a definition. Thus, the objection that many combinations are unnecessary is not valid.

Checkpoint
What is the first reason the marginal commentator gives for objecting to the restriction of definition's purpose?
A) He accepts the restriction
B) He denies that the purpose is restricted to two things
C) He says the purpose is only distinction
D) He says the purpose is only essence

Key Takeaway
The commentator demonstrates that the purpose of definition is not limited to essentials; accidental properties can also be part of a definition, and even if limited, the argument against including them as parts fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the debate over the definition of 'khamr' (wine) in Islamic law, one definition is 'intoxicating beverage made from grapes'. The essential property is intoxication; the accidental property is the source (grapes). Using the commentator's reasoning, argue whether the accidental property can be part of the definition, and under what condition.
?
Evaluate the claim: 'The purpose of definition in fiqh is only to distinguish the definiendum from others.' Give a counterexample from Islamic jurisprudence where knowing an accidental property (e.g., the method of preparation) is beneficial for legal ruling.
